### Post by LaRoza on 2007-12-19
If you own a ThinkPad, you are probably very happy.

The ThinkPad series has been known for "exceptional build quality, system reliability", according to wikipedia, and I support that phrase.

So, what ThinkPads have you owned, own, or wish to own.

If you have any problems or exception stories (good or bad) let us know.

Articles and other interesting ThinkPad related links are welcome.

I recently bought, in October, a ThinkPad R61. I love it so far and hope to for a long time.

It has:

Intel Core 2 Duo Processor
2 GB RAM
160 GB Hard Drive
DVD +RW Drive

It works perfectly with Gutsy, however, Feisty had issues with the DVD drive, but that was fixable.

### Post by toupeiro on 2007-12-19
I've had a T40 and currently have a T60.

I am at a crossroads right now where my job is giving me the option to upgrade, and i have two options:

T61p or the HP 8710w.  As much as I love thinkpads, I am opting for the HP here.  The config I am getting trumps the T61p in every way (CPU, disk options, screen, video card, and ram) and is only 300 dollars more than the best configured t61p I can get my hands on.

It is by no means a knock against Thinkpads,  I know how good they are, and I want to test out HP's mobile workstation line of laptops to see how they compare.

HP will have:
Intel T7800 CPU
8GB of RAM
17" Display (1920x1200)
Nvidia FX 1600m (512MB of RAM)
120GB Sata
DVD/RW, Wireless, all the other standard goodies.

### Post by 50words on 2007-12-19
I have a T43 and love it. I owned a Dell Inspiron for five years before this computer, and replaced two screens, two keyboard, three motherboards, a disk drive, a hard drive, and probably more. I have had my ThinkPad for 2.5 years, and have only replaced a button on the touchpad (too much Morrowind will do that to you). I take my laptop everywhere, and need ThinkPad durability. Nothing else cuts it.

4.5-5 lbs is still too heavy for my liking, though (no, I am not small, I just like to travel light).

Unfortunately, I have an ATI Radeon Mobility video card, which means either screwing around with configuration files and third-party crap, or waiting for better drivers. In 7.10, at least I can use some of the Compiz enhancements.

I am hoping that another six months with the open ATI information will mean a better driver for Hardy Heron. If I can ever get dual monitors working, I can start thinking about ditching Windows entirely.

### Post by steelcap on 2007-12-19
T61 here - Centrino Duo 2.0Ghz - 2Gb Ram - Intel graphics.

My CTO loves HP and always want to buy them when we get notebooks, but I held out for a Thinkpad. After playing with my T61 for about 5 minutes he asked me to order him one too. 

I need XP for my windows sysadmin tools at work, but this thing dual boots Kubuntu like a dream. There are a couple of things that don't work out of the box, but can be fixed in a few minutes (when I get the time) - the page forward/backward buttons and small issues with screen brightness. Wireless works beautifully without any configuration.

### Post by zcal on 2007-12-19
ThinkPad R40
1.8 ghz Celeron
512 mb RAM
20 gb hdd
ATI Mobility M6 LY 16 mb

I bought this on eBay for around $500 a couple of years ago.  I think it must've been owned by a business and licensed to an employee in its previous life...

It's run a gauntlet of Linux installs with me.  Ubuntu Breezy all the way through Gutsy, Gentoo, Zenwalk, Debian Sid, Lenny, and now Etch...even FreeBSD.

I like the ThinkPad's design better than any other laptop's (even Apple's line) just because it's all business.  I've always hated how Dells and Compaqs and HPs look like little spaceships.  ThinkPads are simply elegant, they pack useful features, and they're solid.

Now...if only the previous owner hadn't effectively drained the battery on mine. :(

[IMG]http://www.itechnews.net/wp-content/uploads/2007/09/HP-Compaq-8710w-notebook.jpg[/IMG]

This is not your bargin bin laptop line, much like the thinkpad line is not.   HP Proliants are some of the best servers built, but you wouldn't know it from a crappy presario desktop..


Plus, the 8710w docking station has a built in NAS!  Now that, I don't need... but there is a definate drool factor in effect. :-D

### Post by clayts450 on 2008-02-04
T22 bought for £150 off eBay about 2 years ago. Like a true laptop it lives on my lap whilst I watch telly.

Dual boot Windows 98SE/Ubuntu Gutsy Gibbon
40GB HDD
384MB RAM
900MHz PIII

Ubuntu works fine, if a little sluggish at times, and these are my outstanding issues before I kiss Bill Gates goodbye on this laptop (what a horrible thought) :

- get another 256MB stick of ram to up it to the max of 512
- install a replacement motherboard (acquired for £20, also has a 1 Gig processor on it !) to replace this rapidly failing one (suffers from the blink of death, workround is to bend the base manically on boot up, and it works too !)
- get Ultrabay working again (probably related to motherboard issue)
- get scrap T20/21/22/23 to replace some of the plastics (cracked in all the usual places)
- get to grips with wireless once and for all - got further than ever before using WICD, but still having problems connecting (can see the SSIDs but cannot get connected via WPA) plus Ubuntu completely freezes up if I boot up with the WG511 v1 card in the PCMIA slot
- get the Harmony Remote package working on Ubuntu

That should give me a good few years yet with this beastie - she may be falling apart but man, these things are indestructible :) Will reduce Windows 98SE partition to the smallest possible and run with Ubuntu thereafter. Can't remember the last time I used Windows on this laptop actually...

### Post by haylocki on 2008-02-11
Hi,

> **clayts450 said:**
> 
- get to grips with wireless once and for all - got further than ever before using WICD, but still having problems connecting (can see the SSIDs but cannot get connected via WPA) plus Ubuntu completely freezes up if I boot up with the WG511 v1 card in the PCMIA slot


Have you tried disabling ipv6 to get the wireless going ?

I know the devs frown on this behaviour, but it was the only way I could get my wireless cards to connect using WPA encryption.

Now have wireless on a T21 and 770ed using RT2500 wireless cards, and using the nm-applet. :-)

The easiest way to disable ipv6 is to :

```
sudo gedit  /etc/modprobe.d/blacklist
```

and add the line :

```
blacklist ipv6
```

Save the file and reboot.

if 
```
lsmod |grep ipv6
``` 
does not return anything then ipv6 should be disabled

Does not disable ipv6 for all systems, but works for me, and there are threads about disabling ipv6 in the forums, if you need more help.

HTH, Ian

### Post by Pekkalainen on 2008-02-26
OUCH! I fell and accidently bumped the thread 	#-o

Im in the process of buying a refurbished Thinkpad and have 2 choices. The X40 and the X60.

Which one would you say works best with Ubuntu? Performance is a non-issue for me. Both are strong enough to do what I need. What I wonder is which one has the best compability with GNU/Linux in general?

The X60 has a lot of bells and whistles such as fingerprint reader and other "security" gadgets that I dont really need. The wireless chip seems to be Intel but Im not sure. The price is at roughly $1400 (8995 SEK, whoah the dollar is worthless these days!)

The X40 goes for less than half the price and does not have a bunch of useless gizmos, however it does have 11b/g mini PCI adapter that I presume is an Atheros chip and hence a pain to set up? Also I presume this does not support booting from USB stick? It would kinda suck to have to buy an external CD drive just to install an operating system once or twice :???:

So as I said, which one is best from a compability and ease of use/install perspective? Price and performance is irrelevant.

### Post by Pekkalainen on 2008-03-30
Oh happy day!

I just got the LED indicating wireless state working on my X40 :D
I will post the solution here because google *really* need to index it!

This is how it is done;

fire up ye olde terminal and execute these commands:
```

sysctl -w dev.wifi0.ledpin=1

```
then:
```

sysctl -w dev.wifi0.softled=1

```

Did the LED light up?
Then make it permanent by opening sysctl.conf and paste the 2 lines at the end of it:
```

sudo nano /etc/sysctl.conf

```

That solved half of my only problem with this laptop. How do I make the Fn+F5 combo turn wireless on and off?  :-k

Edit: Should not have cheered so much... upon second reboot (worked after first) the fix doesnt work anymore...

---

### Post by aroth87 on 2008-04-02
> **LaRoza said:**
> Mind telling the rest of us what file this is in?

I never run Compiz for myself, but I sometimes show it to people.

It works out of the box with Hardy beta.

Sure can! I didn't include it in my previous post because I couldn't remember which file it was, but a quick Google search reminded me.

sudo gedit /usr/bin/compiz

Comment out the line that reads:
```
T="$T 8086:2982 8086:2992 8086:29a2 8086:2a02 8086:2a12" # intel 965
```

Everything works fine for me. I had to mess around with VLC to get it to play movies but it works fine now.

Adam

### Post by jennifer_c_richards on 2008-04-22
Hello :)

I received my T61p; 6457 BQG, for the technically minded. The machine has the Penryn T9500, 200gb full disk encryption, 4gb ram, Vista Ultish*t 32bit, etc.

Tried to install Etch on it with some success after I used Envy to install the Nvidia drivers, however after a botched install of Novell SLED [that wiped my Visa - very upset - but good test of the Thinkvantage utilities] I decided to give other distro's a try and after a lot of playing I found Ubuntu Hardy Heron 8.04 and am really delighted with it.

The Nvidia Quadro FX570 worked out of the box as did the Intel wireless adapter, so basically all I need to get started. Found it a little buggy [no surprise] but much better after the updates.

Only problem so far seems to be with the power management which didn't notice my battery running flat and the machine died disgracefully. 

Great distro though, and coming from Debian is ideal, especially for me as I'm pretty much a newbie. I use Grub for the bootloader and I'm very happy with the Debian based distro's are good at finding the other O/S's unlike other type Distro's that can't seem to find them!

Still learning the ropes. In case your interested I've had loads of lappy's but this is my first Thinkpad, and basically I chose it after the most careful consideration, and for my money [sadly a lot of!] I don't think there's another machine that comes close to the T61p Penryn. Very happy!

Only thing is that you don't get bragging rights with the Thinkpad as it looks so dull - but on the plus side I think this makes it less nickable to the average scag-head that may want to remove it from my TLC.

So far I've had it a week and it's survived a car crash where it took the full weight of me and the seat squishing it into the dashboard, so it's pretty tough - an attribute I'd rather not have tested!

So all in all V impressed with the T61p with Ubuntu 8.04. 

I love it when they work out of the box and I would recommend that distro to any T61p owners, it will find Vista and Thinkvantage and boot them both from Grub installed in the MBR - yes you loose the Blue button functionality, but I'm sure it's only a matter of time before some talented Grub hacker makes it work - hint, hint ;) 

[ you can boot the Thinkvantage partition from Grub so you don't loose any functionality ]

Looking forward to installing Ubuntu Studio amd64 next.

Jenny

### Post by Dragonbite on 2008-05-09
Is a Thinkpad really worth the extra cost? And is it fully compatible with Linux or does it still take some hunting-and-pecking for restricted drivers and such?

I'm getting a Thinkpad from work, but I am not allowed to install Linux or anything like that on it (and the entire hard drive is being encrypted so I am not fooling around with it).

---

### Post by jimrz on 2008-05-09
> **Dragonbite said:**
> Is a Thinkpad really worth the extra cost? And is it fully compatible with Linux or does it still take some hunting-and-pecking for restricted drivers and such?

I'm getting a Thinkpad from work, but I am not allowed to install Linux or anything like that on it (and the entire hard drive is being encrypted so I am not fooling around with it).

Both of mine have been and still are worth it. TP's, in general, are very linux friendly , although which wifi and video card options you choose determine if you will need restricted drivers. For example my T42 has:

IBM a/b/g mini pci - Atheros 5212 chipset - works out of the box using the madwifi driver

ATI Radeon mobility 9600 vid - also works out of the box with the open driver, but requires the restricted driver if you want to run desktop effects.

---

### Post by LaRoza on 2008-05-09
> **Dragonbite said:**
> Is a Thinkpad really worth the extra cost? And is it fully compatible with Linux or does it still take some hunting-and-pecking for restricted drivers and such?

I'm getting a Thinkpad from work, but I am not allowed to install Linux or anything like that on it (and the entire hard drive is being encrypted so I am not fooling around with it).

Extra cost? If they are not, then Macs are totally out of the question ;)

ThinkPads start at $536.25 according to the Lenovo site, and that computer is pretty good. 

Plus, they all are robust. They are very strong and will take a beating although I don't recommend one intentionally beat their Thinkpad. 

In addition, the ones with Intel hardware work perfectly with Linux as far as I can tell. Wireless, video, and the trackpad and Trackpoint.

The non Intel models do work with Linux also, but often require enabling the driver for the video I think. 

Thinkpads were the first notebooks to have Linux support from the company.

They are worth every penny and I wouldn't have it any other way. 

My specs (R61i):
[list]
[*] Intel Core 2 Duo
[*] 2 GB RAM
[*] 160 GB hard disk
[*] DVD +RW
[*] PC Card slot, three USB (not all on one side), VGA and ethernet.
[/list]

For less than 1000 USC, that is pretty good, no?

### Post by Whiffle on 2008-05-09
Not to mention, thinkpads have some very handy features that may seem like gimmicks when you're reading the specs, but they do come in handy from time to time.  Things like:

ultrabay hot swappable drive bays, you can put in a battery, extra hard drive, cd/dvd...

thinklight.  Its this little light at the top of the screen that lights up your keyboard, its nice if you're working in the dark.  Also works great as an IM indicator with the pidgin-thinklight plugin.

keyboard with drain holes... need I say more?  

HDAPS- hard drive active protection system.  If you have it running, it parks your hard drive if it detects that the laptop is falling.  Also handy for playing games and other goofy things since the accelerometers also have a joystick output 

tp_smapi - its a linux module that lets you access more about the battery system, including setting charging thresholds to keep your battery happy.  

Yeah, I like my thinkpad.... heck I got it used too.

---

### Post by LaRoza on 2008-05-13
> **50words said:**
> My new ThinkPad arrived today, and WOW, what a DISAPPOINTMENT! It is HUGE! There is at least an inch of wasted space on all sides. The trackpad is tiny--it is smaller than my previous ThinkPad, and nearly useless. It is big, heavy, and will not fit in any of my computer bags.

Perhaps you weren't looking for a thinkpad like computer? The strength and durability of the Thinkpad are probably the cause of the size that you don't like. Remember, the screen doesn't flex and the motherboard won't be bending. I have seen lighter laptops that felt like paper compared my Thinkpad.

My T42 was near-perfect. This is far from it. But I guess the beautiful hardware is why you spend the dough for a Mac. Save $1,000, get a brick.

### Post by archer6 on 2008-05-22
Greetings,

New Ubuntu user here. Just finished getting my ThinkPad R51e set-up with Ubuntu 7.10 Gusty Gibbon. The install went really well, I had a CD with the OS on it. Simply booted from it, and chose install. The only problem I had was with the Wi-Fi. After a few tweaks I finally got it working really well. 

I used my most basic ThinkPad to test Ubuntu and Linux in general to see if I would like it. Here's the very modest configuration of my 14" R51e: 1.5 GHz Celeron, 768 MB ram, 40GB 4200rpm HD. ! About as basic as you can get. In fact when I purchased it, the idea was to buy the  least expensive ThinkPad laptop so I could just toss it in my backpack for weekend use no matter where I was. Therefore saving my prize T60p from being damaged. At that time I had no idea I would be putting Linux on it a year later. 

Speed!.... now here is the shocker (at least for me) with a measly 1.5 Celeron, and 768 of shared memory, this baby is really fast! Even when running disk intensive Firefox on this slow 40GB 4200 rpm HD. 

Now that I have it up, running and stable, it's on to learn what the heck I'm doing.......:)

All I've had to do is replace the BIOS battery--not bad for such an old laptop! I have it running Debian with Openbox and some lightweight applications. Some take a while to start up (PCManFM, Abiword, and others), but that's my fault for picking the large alternatives. I don't use it much at all (it's mainly an experimental linux laptop), so I didn't mind having some larger applications. I opted for Debian over Puppy, DSL, and others so that I could start from scratch. Still, there's a good bit of bloat in Debian that I wasn't fully aware of at the time. Perhaps I'll do Linux From Scratch on it this summer when I have more time.

In addition to that, I've been looking at Thinkpads for a while now... I'm undecided between a T61 and a Macbook. I really like both designs; the Macbook is much simpler and more elegant, while the Thinkpad is much more rugged and durable (and still elegant in its own way). I also enjoy OS X (with its UNIX underpinnings, it's similar to Linux), which I would boot alongside Arch Linux or Ubuntu (the Thinkpad would be Linux-only :)). Very tough decision... :confused: I'll see what happens at Macworld and what happens with the new line of Intel processors.

Thanks for all the great links and info! 
No I have not seen that ThinkPad specific site you linked so that's going to be a great read for me. 

Regarding ThinkPads vs MacBook here is where I may be able to help you.
Short history, I've been using a laptop as my main computer for a decade.

In that period I have had these ThinkPads:
My ThinkPads: 600, 600x | T20, 21, 22, 23 | T30 | T40, 41, 42p, 43 | R51 | Z60m | X60s | T60 | T60p | X300

In that same period I have had these Apple Laptops:
My Apples: PowerBook: 140 | 180c | 540c | 3400 | G3 | G4 | G4 1GHz DVI | G4 17" | MacBook Pro | MacBook | MacBook Pro |

I'm a big Advocate of ThinkPads, while not quite as pleased with Apples. The main reason is that ThinkPads consistently have much better build quality. While Apples look stunning, as the design is very beautiful it's what's under the skin that causes problems. Some models have been very close to the perfect image that Apple portrays. For example my 2002 PowerBook G4 1GHZ Superdrive machine had a very good keyboard, not quite as good as a ThinkPad but very good for an Apple. It had a good display, battery life, and OS X. If is not for Leopard, I would not have a MBP now. I just purchased the latest Model of MBP the one with the new Penryn Chip, and the LED back light. Before the purchase at the Apple store I explained that I wanted to use it to run BootCamp and XP Pro SP3 so that I could run Outlook and BlackBerry Desktop Manager. They assured me that would be no problem, especially since I added 4GB memory. 

So I set it up and found that when booting out of XP to Leopard, the clock would reset itself one day and 7 hours ahead. When I corrected it in Leopard then booted into XP, the same thing occurred requiring me to reset the calendar and clock again. Apple tech support said nothing could be done but I could buy VMware or Parallels and that would fix it. Well I didn't want to run a virtual machine environment but caved in, so that I could run Outlook which I must do for business. After installing VMware the clock problem was fixed. Whew! Now I can use my new MBP.....WRONG! Why? 

Because there is a KNOWN problem that again I was not told about. I travel in my business and meet clients at Starbucks. I use their WiFi constantly as well as having WiFi at home and at the Office. Well, the MBP will not stay connected and drop outs are common place. It's well documented all over the net, however I had no idea prior to experiencing it. I took my 3 day old MBP back to the Apple store, they claimed they never heard of it and gave me a new computer. OK.... so I try that one....same problem. At that point the "Genius" at the bar suggests a Black Mac Book as it's plastic as we know, as opposed to aluminium and he says they are not having a problem. Long story short a week and 2 MacBooks later, same problem. I was so incredibly frustrated at the loss of time and that I could not use one. I really like Leopard and OS X in general and the fact that they have had this WiFi issue, for over TWO years! That's what my research revealed. So I'm certainly not bashing Apple, but providing a true report so as to hopefully save someone else from the same fate. 

Conversely, I've had nothing but really great results from ThinkPads, not only on a personal basis but I have deployed 100's of them in the high-tech company that I founded over a decade ago. When you buy a ThinkPad, you are buying into a very reliable platform with a long history of corporate usage. The web site alone, provides so much info, drivers, tutorials, service videos, you name it. It's vast, comprehensive and everything you want is there. Including all the info on the older models, as IBM still provides the support even though Lenovo has it's name on the machines. It remains a IBM/Lenovo partnership of sorts. I have never been let down my a ThinkPad and they are continuing that tradition with the Lenovo transition. Many people ask... are the Lenovo ThinkPads as good as the IBM ones... to which I say absolutely. I have compared them very carefully and they are doing a very good job. 

Finally there is a completely new line coming out, one of which is already here and that is the X300. The line-up will be as follows:

1) X200   12"
2) X300   13"
3) X400   14"
4) X500   15"

I have an X300 and it's fantastic, the first quantum leap in design for some time. Built quality is vault like. The legendary keyboard is there, the display is brilliant, and the overall experience is exemplary. The very moment the X500 is released I will have one of those as I do like as much display area as possible as I'm a designer / creator / software engineer. 

Hope this info helps you.

Cheers!

via my Ubuntu ThinkPad....:)

### Post by archer6 on 2008-05-23
> **DeliriousNor said:**
> Thanks for the fast reply:D I have already added more memory to mye Thinkpad and it currently has 1,5 gb of ram. I am pretty sure that is highest amount of ram it supports, I am not a 100 % sure though. Does anybody know? I got the model with 1,5 ghz prosessor so I do not know how much ram that would be reasonable in combination with a 1,5 ghz processor.

The X41 provides much better performance than your average ultraportable due to the fact it has a low-voltage 1.50 GHz Pentium M that uses the latest Intel 915 chipset.  According to Intel there's about a 10% performance gain from the older 855 chipset. It also supports and takes advantage of a full 2GB ram. 

The hard drive speed of 4200RPM is often pointed to as being too slow. And the problem with the hard drive size on the X41 (1.8") is that there is no upgrading to a faster hard drive.  With _extra_ RAM_ that will provide _less need to hit the hard drive_, you'll be able to overcome performance issues, and with the included processor, the speed increase by installing a total of 2GB of ram is astounding. 

It's important to know that by having a pair (same capacity, and speed) of 1GB Ram modules, the computer will run at it's fastest and utilize the full 2GB. Whereas with a 1GB module and a 512MB module as you have now the "actual" performance is equivalent to about 1.1GM of ram. All due to the way the processor "sees" and manages the available memory. So you are actually slowing the performance with mis matched ram. 

DDR2-PC4200 is the proper spec ram for your machine. Thus you can easily remove the 512 module, and replace it with a 1GB module for just $21.99. You would then be enjoying the fastest speed from your lapotp. 

This is Crucial memory which is top of the line and on sale here: [Newegg]("http://www.newegg.com/Product/Product.aspx?Item=N82E16820145097")

I run this setup in my X41 and it's fantastic. 

Hope this helps

Cheers!

---

> Does any of you use your Thinkpad for developing applications?

What are thinkpad benefits over Dell and HP?

I have used ThinkPads for a decade. I use my ThinkPad T60p for 3D design, animation, modeling and rendering, the p series are IBM/Lenovo's designation for workstation class laptops. I use Autodesk Maya, and Inventor applications. This resource intensive work that a Dell simply could not handle, or at least for very long. The Dell product line is a line of mostly consumer based laptops, and the ones that are designed to handle business tasks are more oriented to your average set of apps like MS Office, etc. Nothing against Dell, but they simply cannot measure up. ThinkPads have full size keyboards that are very robust, have very comfortable solid build quality and keys that are designed for those who touch type. The system boards, the hard drives, pci boards, everything about them is top of the line. There is simply no comparison. When it comes to support they have a vast array of drivers, and other software components that go back as far as ten years. When you buy a ThinkPad it's designed to last a very long time. The structural rigidity is beyond what any other laptop offers. These are very strong, durable and made from the finest materials. No cheap weak plastics to crack, or distort. BIOS updates are regularly available where I recently read there is a class action lawsuit against HP, as they have no provisions for updating the BIOS as well as some other critical software elements. If you go to Lenovo online and read about ThinkPads you will come away with an entirely different perspective and a new appreciation for these world class business machines.

---

### Post by archer6 on 2008-06-05
> **50words said:**
> My new ThinkPad arrived today, and WOW, what a DISAPPOINTMENT! It is HUGE! There is at least an inch of wasted space on all sides. The trackpad is tiny--it is smaller than my previous ThinkPad, and nearly useless. It is big, heavy, and will not fit in any of my computer bags.

I will happy pay the $232 restocking fee to send this brick back to Lenovo, and Lenovo has lost a customer today, not because of their service, which is fantastic, but because of the poor product.

Sounds like perhaps it was chosen in haste, without looking at the specs. I have a ThinkPad that only weighs 2.7 lbs, is less than an inch thick and has great battery life. 

As far as track pad size, I have found that the reason it's not _giant sized_ is because it's of such high quality that it is very efficient and one does not have to move a finger 3 or 4 inches to get it to respond. It is very accurate and responds immediately. To judge any laptop the moment it's taken out of the packaging and only used for one day, then claim it's a poor product suggests more about the way it's being evaluated, than the truth about the product, no matter what brand. Somehow I think that when one looks around to see all the fortune 500 hundred companies that rely on ThinkPads, as well as so many other businesses, they must have something going for them. They have been the gold standard in business before Dell or HP even thought of having laptops made by ODM mfgs for them.

### Post by archer6 on 2008-06-18
> **cookieofdoom said:**
> If you don't have to have the CD ROM drive to carry around with you (or you can use an external), and the higher resolution screen. The X61 is a great little machine, and it's a lot cheaper.. The docking bay thing has a DVD ROM built in.

I could not agree more. I have used an X41 for a long time, then 18 months ago I bought my X60s and could not be happier. I have large hands, and yet the 97% of full size keyboard is wonderful. So much larger than that on any other 12.1" laptop. I like to carry it with the slim battery which does not protrude out the back or down below the computer, it's flush and keeps the laptop very thin and light. 

Here are the specs of my X60s (X61, is nearly the same)
I weighed mine on a calibrated industrial scale at work and it's only 2.3lbs. It also has a small footprint: 10.5" x 8.3" x .83 - 1.00" thick. I have the "UltraLight" TFT display that is 20% brighter and 25% lighter than the standard X60s. The magnesium housing is ultra strong and also reduces the weight. I have the low voltage 1.66GHz CoreDuo, which benchmarks at 30% faster than the 1.5GHz regular voltage chip that was in my X41. In addition I regularly get over 5 hours on Transatlantic flights, whereas the X41 only returned 2 hours 45 min. I loaded it up with 2GB ram which makes this one very fast, quite, compact, laptop. 

The only exception is it lacks Ubuntu.....soon to be remedied by yours truly.....:)..... as it has 100GB 7200rpm HDD, so lots of room.

Current pricing on the X61 is far below what I paid for mine and for those of you who do not _have_ to have the X300, I would highly suggest you take one of these into consideration. They are a great bargain at this present moment.

Yes, I had the exact same problem on my R51, it ran flawlessly on 7.10 Gutsy. I performed the upgrade and then experienced this same issue you described. ### Post by archer6 on 2008-06-28
> **50words said:**
> Yup. Do you know what the update will be? Just specs, or also changes to the form factor? LED screens?
First, looking at the big picture they are updating all ThinkPads in terms of a completely new design, housings and all. This is the first time in many years that ThinkPads have been completely redesigned. 

I'm including a link that's a bit old, but the only one so far that shows pictures of the prototypes. This link calls them X series in the various sizes. The latest I have is that the 14 & 15 inch models will be called T400 & T500. They are also reverting back to the 4x3 aspect ratio (optional on some models)(non-widescreen) for those of us like myself that are software engineers and prefer the taller standard format display. 

Also there is rumor that the IPS display technology which IBM (yes prior to Lenovo) calls FlexView, will return. The T60 I'm writing this on is one of the last to have Flexview, before it was discontinued due to a screen shortage. The advantage for those of you who have not heard of IPS is that it provides a very wide viewing angle which is great if several people are viewing the display at the same time. 

Here's the Link [New ThinkPads]("http://gizmodo.com/378504/lenovos-entire-new-thinkpad-line-leaked-x300-gets-siblings")

Cheers!

It does not affect the MacBooks as they are plastic, so no interference as the aluminum cased MBPs suffer from. So I returned the MBP to my local Apple store where they waived the 10% restocking fee as they promised they would if the WiFi did not work. I'm hoping that the upcoming new MacBook / MacBook Pro models that are rumored to be released at Mac World SF this year will finally have that problem resolved. They will never replace my ThinkPad but I like to have some variety in what I use as my personal laptop for fun stuff.

### Post by archer6 on 2008-06-28
> **LaRoza said:**
> That is good news.

What would I be interested in? I have four computers, including two notebooks (R61i, and iBook). I'd probably want to get something I could really show off, perhaps an X series. I don't see the point of desktop replacements and a full features small computer would be hot (in a good way)

From my perspective there are three choices that might suit you well. It comes down to your preference of size of display and overall size and footprint of the laptop. 

I have an X60s which is very small, thin, and just 2.3lbs. The display is 12.1" and it's the computer that I carry when I don't want to carry anything larger. The battery fits flush with the back and the bottom so it does not protrude at all. What I particularly like about my X60s besides it very diminutive size, is the all magnesium housing which gives it great strength and makes it very rugged. I routinely put it in a Waterfield sleeve and pop it into my backpack. You hardly know it's there. Also the power supply has very small & light brick.  

They can be had new for about 40% less than I paid for mine. They are selling briskly since it's the end of the line for these. Lenovo is clearing them out, as the new X200 will be out shortly. Between those two the X61s (same as my X60s)and the X200, the difference will be small enough, that one might want to take advantage of the very good prices on the X61s, now. 

If you want a larger display in a form factor that is still small then you might want the X300. Although I have both of these listed below, I still prefer the X60s for it's smaller footprint. The extra 2" width and 1" depth of the X300 does make a difference when carrying it. 

The Dimensions are as follows:

**X300** 12.4" x 9.1" x 0.73" - 0.92"

**X61s** 10.5" x 8.3" x 0.83" - 1.0"

If you want a larger display in a form factor that is still small then you might want the X300. Although I have both of these listed below, I still prefer the X60s for it's smaller footprint. The extra 2" width and 1" depth of the X300 does make a difference when carrying it. 

The Dimensions are as follows:

**X300** 12.4" x 9.1" x 0.73" - 0.92"

**X61s** 10.5" x 8.3" x 0.83" - 1.0"

### Post by archer6 on 2008-06-28
> **50words said:**
> I have a T43. Do I have two fans, or only one? How can I tell if it/they are running?
The T43 has one fan. 

You are very lucky that yours is operating properly. There were many T43's, a huge number with noisy fans, as a result of a problem with the embedded controller program. What was happening was that it would adjust the fan speed (what it's designed to do) up and down too frequently, as a result it caused a surge in the fan speed, which in turn caused quite a bit of noise. It's normal mapping was to maintain a certain constant fan speed according to load, not the increase and immediate decrease in rpm that those users experienced. IBM created several different versions of the controller program in an effort to arrest the problem but the effectiveness was marginal at best. 

I had the problem in my T43, the only reason I finally got it under control within reason was that I'm fortunate enough to have a tremendous amount of patience and desire to stick with it, as I enjoy the learning opportunity that those truly difficult issues present. In my case I worked on it on and off for months as the different updates were released. What was particularly unique about this issue what that as it ended up there was not a single solution for all users. Vexing indeed. 

Therefore if yours is quiet consider yourself very lucky. As far as how to tell if it's running, if you put your ear to the exhaust vents you may hear it. Another option, if you cannot hear it which is possible (some are incredibly quiet) is to take a tissue and hang it over the vent and it should move just enough to indicate the fan is running. Remember that it only runs when the system is being taxed, as the thermal management of the T series beginning with the T4x models is so effective that the fan does not run during basic usage such as working in Word, or on the web. 

Cheers

### Post by archer6 on 2008-06-28
> **Vorian said:**
> I have a T61 and <3 it very much.

I find the T60 and T61 models to be my current favorites. 

They are very well sorted out, and run well under any circumstances. I have a tremendous amount of hours on my T60, more than I've put on any of the prior models and this one is stellar. One of the features that I have on the T60 (which sadly is no longer available) is the display technology known as IPS, which in turn IBM/Lenovo calls Flexview. For those who may not know what this is, it creates a display that has an exceptionally wide and deep viewing angle. It's great when you want to show others what's being displayed. They can either stand or sit next to the computer with you behind the keyboard and see it as well as the user. It also offers better color saturation, and color balance. It was an optional display that was only offered on a few of the T60 and T60p models with the conventional 4x3 aspect ratio (not widescreen). For that reason, as well as others I plan on using my T60 for a long time.

Cheers

### Post by LaRoza on 2008-06-29
> **frustschieber said:**
> T61p running Kubuntu 8.04
not functioning: Thinkvantage, fingerprint, external Monitor

The Thinkvantage button boots a seperate partition with a version of PC-DOS on it for recovery. Obviously, when you install Linux, you don't have that option anymore...

Fingerprinter readers can work, but they are not secure. Don't use them if you value security.

For the external monitor, try starting the computer with the monitor plugged in. There is probably an easier way, but that works.

### Post by archer6 on 2008-06-29
> **zmjjmz said:**
> I was setting up my mom's new T61 (XP Pro), and I messed around a bit with Thinkvantage.
It seems kinda useless, it's just a maintenance sidebar.
The Thinkvantage system with its own Button is a useful multi function tool. For experienced computer users, initially it does seem unimpressive, however its target user is beginners. As you noticed when pushed with the computer booted, the Thinkvantage button brings up a window which offers the user many utilities. Power manager, mouse preferences for the track stick, track pad, and many other settings & systems. Backup etc. So indeed it does offer a lot of functionality if one takes the time to explore all it does. 

Next up is perhaps its best advantage & function. Usage is as follows. When booting up when the splash screen appears you are prompted to press the Thinkvantage button (if you have a problem) after which a window appears that gives you a few choices. One is to restore your computer while saving your files. Other choices give you the ability to choose exactly what you wish to save. Finally the most useful of all is if the entire OS has crashed, it will access the "hidden partition" and perform a complete, automatic reformat of the HDD & then a clean install of the OS, all drivers & all preloaded software. Once its done, the computer is returned to the "as new" out of the box state. Just like when you first unpacked it to set it up. Of particular value is that the need for restore discs is gone, it also will rid the hard drive of the most malicious worms, or other viruses. A very handy tool. Also the entire procedure is very fast, in less than 90 minutes you are done, as good as new. 

sent via: BlackBerry 

Cheers

---

### Post by archer6 on 2008-06-29

> The Thinkvantage button boots a seperate partition with a version of PC-DOS on it for recovery.
For clarification the The recovery partion contains PC-DOS_and_ XPpro (or Vista) so that once it finishes , you are completely restored to the way the computer came out of the box when new.

### Post by archer6 on 2008-06-29
> **jimrz said:**
> Have had my T42 for several years and had no such problems, nor have I seen complaints about them. It's a great machine ... enjoy.

As a longtime ThinkPad user of every model in the T-series lineup I simply cannot say enough good things about the T-42 model. Even better than my T-43. My T-42 served me so well, over an incredible amount of hours, that I kept it when I upgraded to the T60. 

I would encourage you to enjoy yours, and onto it as long as possible as it is truly one finest of the T-Series. Also another thing that I really like about IBM/Lenovo is the service and parts availability. IBM still does the servicing for Lenovo in their long time service facility in Atlanta. The is one of the best depot service facilities around. I know, as I've been there as a guest and enjoyed a tour of the facility. They keep parts in stock or available through special order for at least 10 years for each T-Series. Thus you can keep that baby going for a very long time. 

They also offer an unlisted (unofficial) rebuilding service for all ThinkPads. If one wishes to take advantage of it, as your computer gets older, well out of warranty and begins to develop some normal wear and tear issues with hardware, the service works as follows: I will use myself as an example. I have this old T22 which I bought new in Mar of 2001. I really like it (I'm such a geek, I get attached to certain favorite ThinkPads) so I called in, explained that my T22 keyboard was worn and noisy and that the hard drive was also beginning to knock. The person I spoke with said they have a minimum charge of $225 to look at and repair one major component on an old machine like that. Then he asked if there was anything else that was worn. I mentioned that several pixels had gone bad, and the hinges were getting loose. His response, really surprised me. With great courtesy he said, well just make a list of everything you can think of and we will check it out for you at no extra charge. They sent out a box, I sent in the computer and big list. Much to my surprise three days later I had the computer back, along with a bill for exactly $225 which was his minimum charge. Not only did they get it turned around really quickly, and did not charge me for shipping. But when I took it out of the box it was as clean as new. I looked at the invoice and they had replaced the Keybord, Hard drive, display - including cover and hinges, they put an all new palm rest, and surrounding plastic so that not only was the keyboard new but so were on/off button, the other buttons on that panel and all the plastic that surrounded it. The put in a new motherboard, bus, cooling fan, new ports, everywhere I looked I saw new parts. I was shocked, and needless to say very happy. All for $225, my entire computer was rebuilt to new condition. Simply amazing as it was 6 years old (this was done in 2007). Needless to say, once again I was reminded at why it pays to be a loyal ThinkPad customer.

### Post by archer6 on 2008-07-01
> **hvac3901 said:**

My first experience with lenovo was not so good, but i will say it was more of a product and website issue than the laptop itself WHICH I WILL SAY HAS BEEN IMPERVIOUS to issues.



Just a bit of background about the IBM/Lenovo transition:

I do understand your concern with Lenovo. I have done a lot of research on this. Simply because of what a ThinkPad represents to me, as it's been the cornerstone of my business/personal computing for over a decade. I don't use a desktop so a ThinkPad is mission critical for my work. Also my work ThinkPad is the workstation class T60p loaded with all the memory, fastest processor, etc as the type of work I do demands a lot of resources. I always wondered what I would do if suddenly Lenovo began cutting corners and building an inferior product. Especially since I must have a workstation class laptop, not a consumer level machine.  After buying and thoroughly testing the majority of the competing laptop brands & models, frankly I found nothing even close. 

Before_ the Lenovo acquisition of the ThinkPad division of IBM, they (Lenovo) were making _all_ ThinkPads_ for IBM. This is unlike so many of the other companies that buy their laptops from more than one ODM (Original Design Manufacturer) manufacturer who in turn labels the laptop for whomever it's for, such as Dell, HP, etc. Certain models of Dell laptops are made by one ODM, where other models of Dell laptops are made by another ODM. This also changes from year to year. With Lenovo building all of IBM's ThinkPads and only ThinkPads they had & currently have the best track record. Only having one OEM (Original Equipment Manufacturer)company to build for, they have their focus on ThinkPads exclusively. 

Then moving on to the question of service, Lenovo not having any service facilities, contracted with IBM to continue to service ThinkPads. So, we still have the best of both worlds. The original company still making ThinkPads, and IBM still servicing them. Thus only two things have changed 1) Ownership of the brand 2) Lenovo now makes the decisions as to R&D, and future products. The last "True ThinkPad designed by IBM" is the 60 series. That's why you see in my signature line that I have four. I was not taking any chances and bought one of each of the form factors, types, sizes, and configurations that I use. That way in the event that  the next model that Lenovo would have full over, was not up to par, I would have the last "Pure IBM ThinkPads" to count on for several years. Thus far the 61 series has been a "carry over" machine of sorts, while Lenovo designed the all new upcoming X & T series. These represent the first time the entire line of ThinkPads have been completely redesigned. These are "Pure Lenovo". So time will tell. 

As far as getting _your_ T22_ repaired / rebuilt by IBM. It's been a year since I took advantage of this service, and I can only hope it's still available. Lenovo has made numerous policy changes, which have so far only had a minor impact on the line. I will be interested in seeing how you do, and I'm hoping the same service is still available. Just a "tip" for you. When you call in to tech support, I would not begin by saying "I understand that I can have my entire computer rebuilt for the minimum basic charge" as that's not "Officially" how it works. The approach I used was as I mentioned earlier. Have just one or two "problems" your calling in on, and asking for an estimate on. The sense I got was the person on the phone has quite a bit of latitude, I just happened to get a good guy that "led me through" the process. I explained my one major issue, and mentioned that I had a second problem, because I approached him in a very courtesy positive way (I love my ThinkPad, blah...blah...) he was eager to work with me and "offer" what I could get if I simply sent in a list.  He was obviously sharing with me in a round about way, what he might be able to do for me. Thus he didn't commit to anything beyond the original item but encouraged me to send that list in. Once they had the computer and the list, they simply fixed everything they found wrong, and got it right back to me. So hopefully you will have the same experience. 

Cheers....;)

### Post by Murrquan on 2008-07-02
I recently bought a refurbished Thinkpad R51 from Tigerdirect.com, apparently on behalf of a third-party supplier called Metro-something. When it arrived it had no software on CD save for a copy of Lotus SmartSuite (meaning I had to create a seven-CD set of restore discs via an onboard utility, which restore Windows XP for dual-booting *and* lots of trashware that came installed with it). Plus, its battery was all but drained. It only lasts a half-hour.

Shortly after I got it the 's' key snapped off. I kept putting it back into place, but after replacing it multiple times just this morning and not being able to get it to stay there I stopped. I am now typing this on a USB keyboard.

I have called the third-party supplier. They say it's not covered by my warranty.

The Thinkpad itself looks like a nice machine. The Thinklight and the brightness and volume controls work right out of the box. The Radeon 9000 works out of the box in Fedora, and I'm told there's a workaround for Ubuntu. And the Trackpoint's kind of fun to play with, although even after following the instructions on Thinkwiki I haven't been able to get middle-button scrolling to work. I guess I'm kind of an Xorg newb.

I don't think it's the Thinkpad's fault that I'm having so much trouble with it. I guess it just got worn out when it was in some corporate fleet. And I made the mistake of buying from a lousy supplier (that wants to charge a big restocking fee to take it back). I'm just kind of frustrated right now. It doesn't help that I don't have a backup PC, and that I use this mini USB keyboard for a game console as well.

Sorry to dampen the mood in here. >.>

### Post by LaRoza on 2008-07-02
> **Dragonbite said:**
> I was thinking about that, but I haven't been able to get in touch with him yet.  I'll let you know!

Besides the grammatical error, there is an historical error:

[http://www.strongblade.com/prod/sba-gladiuslegionnaire.html](http://www.strongblade.com/prod/sba-gladiuslegionnaire.html)

> 
It's no wonder that a civilization so in love with stabbing one another came up with one of the most perfect thrusting swords in history.

The Romans didn't invent it, in fact, they acknowledged that openly. "Gladius" is just the Latin word for "sword", as I stated earlier, it is a "Gladius Hispaniensis" that is what the Romans used (that is what they called it), meaning "Spanish Sword". They got the design from the people they fought in Spain (although who and exactly when is a little unknown). The Romans changed the design over time (to make it shorter and broader) but it was basically the same sword design.

The sword wasn't just a stabbing sword, it was a great slashing sword, and the Greek found that out the hard way... (The Greeks were used to fighting with spears and were appalled at the carnage left behind after a battle, forget the battle's name, but I'll get back to you on that)

### Post by archer6 on 2008-07-02
> **Murrquan said:**
> 
Shortly after I got it the 's' key snapped off. 

One of many advantages of owning a ThinkPad is that parts are readily available and very reasonably priced. Below is a link to a page on the Lenovo/IBM site that you enter your model number and serial number into, then it looks up exactly what parts are in your ThinkPad and from what supplier, and gives you a number to order. If you have any trouble finding what you want, you can simply call tech support with the same info and they will assist you. There are three different manufacturers of keyboards for ThinkPads. Thus with your info they will find the exact one that you need. 

I have replaced a keyboard before on a machine that someone must have played football with, as I've never had to replace a single keyboard on any of the 200+ ThinkPads that I deploy in my company. Anyway, this was for a friend, he ordered the keyboard and brought the computer to me. It took just 10 minutes (truly) to remove 5 screws from the bottom side of the computer, open the display lift the loose keyboard, unplug the connection, install the new one and put the screws back in. 

ThinkPads are designed to be very easy for the user to service and repair which is a real plus. Anything you could possibly need is available separately as well, so your not stuck buying whole assemblies of components when you only need something small. Take some time to look around the web site and you will be amazed. They have a terrific site which will provide you with any of the hardware, system software, drivers, BIOS updates, you name it. Also there is a place on the site where you can sign up and they will send you any updates that apply, even if your computer is not brand new. They have everything one could possibly need on the web site. The only challenge is being patient until you become accustomed to the site. They have been making ThinkPads for so long, in thousands of different configurations that the site is huge. 

_[ThinkPad Parts Lookup]("http://www-307.ibm.com/pc/support/site.wss/product.do?template=%2Fpartslookup%2FpartsLookup.vm&sitestyle=lenovo")_

Cheers......:)

### Post by LaRoza on 2008-07-02
> **Dragonbite said:**
> Weren't the Romans more like Kingons, where they usually conquered and then stole ideas from the Greeks and others more than come up with their own? (hey.. that's starting to sound like a company I've heard of ....)

No, the Romans were a very practicle people. They had little inovation on their own and made use of other culture's ideas (didn't steal them).

Look at the Flavian Amphitheatre, guess how long that took to build? (And looks at its very advanced features). They Romans were masters at building and mass production. They took the temple of Athena from a design in Greece and built it bigger and better in less time in Rome.

Romans respected the artistic achievements of the Greeks and often studied the Greeks and used Greek teachers.

### Post by Dragonbite on 2008-07-03
> **LaRoza said:**
> Besides the grammatical error, there is an historical error:

[http://www.strongblade.com/prod/sba-gladiuslegionnaire.html](http://www.strongblade.com/prod/sba-gladiuslegionnaire.html)



The Romans didn't invent it, in fact, they acknowledged that openly. "Gladius" is just the Latin word for "sword", as I stated earlier, it is a "Gladius Hispaniensis" that is what the Romans used (that is what they called it), meaning "Spanish Sword". They got the design from the people they fought in Spain (although who and exactly when is a little unknown). The Romans changed the design over time (to make it shorter and broader) but it was basically the same sword design.

The sword wasn't just a stabbing sword, it was a great slashing sword, and the Greek found that out the hard way... (The Greeks were used to fighting with spears and were appalled at the carnage left behind after a battle, forget the battle's name, but I'll get back to you on that)

My friend responded to my question and offers this link which was his original write-up:
[[COLOR="Blue"]http://www.strongblade.com/history/romangladius.html[/COLOR]]("http://www.strongblade.com/history/romangladius.html"). 

I'm not going to get into any arguments (I'm way out of my league here) but I know he does his research and you have obviously done your research on it so it may be better for you to email him (via the webmaster).  

You can PM me too.

---

### Post by conundrumx on 2008-07-08
I was under the impression the T4x series had heat management issues.  I know I had a T42 (I think) that was bricked due purely to poor thermal design by IBM.  The graphics card was connected by BGA, but enough heat was generated without being properly dispersed that over time the card actually shifted off where it should be, thus bricking the laptop.

That said, I am quite happily using a T61p to type this post.

---

### Post by archer6 on 2008-07-08
> **conundrumx said:**
> I was under the impression the T4x series had heat management issues.  I know I had a T42 (I think) that was bricked due purely to poor thermal design by IBM.


Not sure where you heard this but it's untrue. There was a batch of ThinkPads manufactured and shipping at the end of the T42 run and beginning of T43 production that were assembled with fans the were out of spec, from one of IBM's suppliers. All owners that registered their computers were notified, and sent new ThinkPads. It was a very impressive move on IBM's part, instead of asking people to send them in and be without while they were being repaired. I know, as I was one of those people. I purhased the T43 the day it was released on got one from that batch. 

Prior to that, I purchased a T42p also on the day it was released, and used that laptop for a year during which it became my favorite out of the ThinkPads I had used to date (which was one new model per year) and I still use it today as a secondary machine. It's one of my two favorites in the period of over ten years with ThinkPads, and it's second only to my T60 and T60p models which I am currently using. The P model seeing the heaviest usage as it's for design modeling, rending, and some other scientific apps that demand the resources. Then my all around laptop (as I do everything via computer) is my T60 which is my all purpose laptop. All three of these run fast, cool, stable, and have exceptionally perfect reliability (read zero problems). So yes, bad fans, but no, not bad design. All one has to do is to take apart a ThinkPad and put it next to a disassembled Dell, Hp, Acer, Sony, Toshiba, Asus or Fujitsu. I know, as I have done this comparison. Being an advocate of laptops, technology, and hardware, I've personally owned, operated and inspected all of the above and at a glance when looking at the component layout, design and build quality, ThinkPads stand out as the remarkable machines they are.

---

### Post by Killer_B on 2008-07-28
Since late 2007, I've used 7.10 -> 8.04 on an old Thinkpad x23. I've managed to get pretty much everything working on it, even hardware acceleration on the radeon mobility 7000 video...Though it's small amount of memory limits me from really doing anything extravagant with it, 3d wise. I ought to get a T60 sometime this week, local sale...Pretty maxed out hardware-wise, for a fairly decent price. ($530, still has warranty til early 2010) A little disappointed that it's only got x1300 radeon graphics, but it ought to do. I may look at putting Mint on this one, in light of some of the comments that I've seen about this model and 8.04, with regards to overheating/shutdowns...Guess it's still undecided whether it's a hardware issue, or a kernel/ACPI issue.

Honestly, I'm amazed at just how well 8.04 runs on a T60. I fully expected to have a few issues, and yet had none! Everything worked perfectly after the install. No tweaking or bug fixing required. Installed in under an hour!

IMHO Unbuntu 8.04 is absolutely the best place for a beginner to start. 
Once you have it up and running then you can browse around the various parts of this forum for info. There is a lot to be learned at a pace you choose, as it's so easy to use once installed, it's fun, logical, and best of all free. I only use Windows for just few must have apps for work. Eventually once I become more knowledgeable about Ubuntu, I fully expect to be able to migrate to Linux for all my work as well. Thereby finally giving windows the heave ho!

### Post by flipteo on 2008-08-06
I just got a t42 (PM 1.7, 512RAM, 40gig hdd, dvd rom) yesterday.  For months my only computer was an ancient thinkpad i-series with a celeron 433 and a whopping 32mb of ram running windows 98.  2004 technology sure beats 1998 technology.  

I briefly had a latitude d600 about a month ago.  I sold it because of the incredible heat issues.  On a hot day the left palm rest would almost literally slowly burn my wrist.  It was a nice computer otherwise, but that's a definite deal breaker.  I got a hundred bucks more than what I paid for it.  I decided this time I'd do a lot more research.  The t42 won.  I love the build quality.  The metal hinges especially.  It just seems to have had a lot of thought put into it overall.  Unlike that dell d600 I had, I can't believe Dell didn't go bankrupt over that one.  There's probably a hundred threads about the heat issue on the Dell site forum alone.  

My t42 came without an OS.  I was a little nervous about taking the linux plunge, especially considering I'd have no safety net.  But it's working out well.  I've only had to do minor tweaking.

Both the touchpad and the trackpoint are operational, but they don't seem quite right.  They are a little erratic.

Also, I'm kinda bummed about not being able to get the juicy desktop effects to work yet.  I have the radeon 7500 card, so I don't know if that will ever be solvable.

I haven't tried the wireless yet.  Nor have I tried a dvd.  I did some tweaking to the xorg file from info on thinkwiki, and after that youtube vids looked much better, so I'm eager to see what a dvd looks like.

I'm totally new to linux, so knowing what app's to get is going to be a process.  First up is trying to find a minimalist music player with good sound manipulation features (is it really that hard to add a bloody equalizer when programming a player?  I'm referring to rythmbox here).  I love the 'pre-amp' deally in winamp, I want to find a gnome player with something like that.  Next up after that will be finding a good p2p app.  Then after that (once all the distractions are set up :)), I'll need to find a html/text editor, something along the lines of notepad++.

Btw, why did IBM stop putting on those little flip out legs at the back of their laptops to prop them up?  My i-series has them.  I find it makes typing for long periods more comfortable.

### Post by ranchersd on 2008-08-10
Just an FYI for those thinking of purchasing a new laptop from lenovo. The Reliability has dropped of significantly in the last few years. I am I.T. for my company and we have 20+ ibm/lenovo laptops. More then half have been in to the Lenovo service shop for one thing or another. Most that have been in the maintenance shop once have needed to go again. Lenovo's ability to resolve problems the first time is embarrassing low in my personal experience. I have a couple laptops that have been in for servicing 3,4, and 5 times. One would think that at some point, it would be financially beneficial for Lenovo to replace a laptop that they are unable to fix. On the contrary. They will continue to replace defective parts with defective parts in some cases, quite content with their poor customer service until your warranty expires. Then you have to pay for their slip-shod repairs out of pocket (to the tune of $600 for a mobo [and memory which tested fine] like we did last week.

Bottom line is that I will not recommend the purchase of another lenovo laptop given my experience over the last 3 years. Unfortunately, my executives go around my recommendations and buy these laptops and then throw their arms up when they break. Last month, one of my execs bought the W series. Lo and behold, it had problems 3 days after opening the box. His answer? Buy another one so he could work with one good laptop while the other is in the shop. Talk about conditioning and preparation for disappointment.

I have no brand to recommend as the few toshibas, HPs, and Sonys have been spotty also when it come to reliability. A real shame because the IBM used to be the gold standard. Now I would have a hard time thinking that any laptop maker could survive making a worse product than Lenovo. 

Just my humble opinion from personal observations and experiences.

Lastly, I have loaner laptops to accommodate my staff when their regular laptop is in the repair cycle. If you do not have the same luxury, I would be especially cautious before buying lenovo until they (a) improve their product and (b) improve their repair facility philosophy.

(Name available on request)
San Diego, CA

HP will have:
Intel T7800 CPU
8GB of RAM
17" Display (1920x1200)
Nvidia FX 1600m (512MB of RAM)
120GB Sata
DVD/RW, Wireless, all the other standard goodies.

I've used a T42, T43, T60, T61 and T61p at work.  I own a T43.  In all honesty, they all work great with Ubuntu.  I wish I could get my old T43 back at work.  My T61 has had 2 motherboards replaced, and the T61's have proven unstable with AT&T WAN and Windows Vista.  The T61p's have had multiple motherboards, one internal WAN card, 2 hard drives and 1 monitor replaced (this is out of 7 total units).  Most all of them have had the top bezel piece replace and are all used for light office work.  Still, I've grown to like the docks so we continue purchasing them.


On another note: 
My personal T43 keeps showing
"thinkpad_acpi: CMOS NVRAM (0) and EC (2) do not agree on display brightness level" 
when I type dmesg.  :( And in fact, brightness levels are inconsistant and may dim during usage.

All other hardware has been very well supported (I don't use the fingerprint reader though because it doesn't work out of the box and it's not critical for me to get it working).  Ubuntu (currently 8.04.1) is (and has always been) very zippy on my T43 and I am rather attached to this notebook.  I just wish IBM would take the line back...I believe Lenovo has dropped lowered the standard for Thinkpads.


T43 Specs
Intel Pentium M 1.73GHz
1GB OCZ PC4200 
64GB Transcend IDE Solid State Disk (4.5 - 6hrs on 6 Cell battery using powertop)
14.1" Monitor with integrated Intel Graphics (works great with Compiz)
I've also done the cooling mod described at [ThinkWiki]("http://www.thinkwiki.org/wiki/How_to_build_a_T43_southbridge_cooler") so I don't burn any important parts.

### Post by KWM987 on 2008-08-12

sent via BlackBerry

IdeaPad Y510, 

Got the machine for the parents when it was on clearance for $400 at the local OfficeDepot that was going out, to replace a old Toshiba Satellite running 7.10 that had the screen blowup.  It seems to be a ThinkPad in drag really:

IdeaPad Y510 15.4"
Intel Core 2 Duo 1.83Ghz
3 GB Ram
160GB HDD
Integrated 965 graphics
Intel 3965b/g wifi

Overall, it looks like they took a basic T61 or R61, stripped alot of the business and toughening features, and replaced them with a bit of polish and flash that the mass consumers will go for-- it's got alot of lights/LED's on it, touch sensitive controls, gloss finish on the screen, face recognition camera, 4.1 Dolby speakers build in (a marketing gimmick, but they are the best sounding laptop speakers I have ever heard), matte/soft touch finish on the trackpad and palm area, and a faux cloth weave finish on the lid.  Not exactly what you want or need in a work horse, but perfect for mainstream users.  Faults I can see is they cheaped out on the AC adapter and CD drive, which don't feel (or sound) as quality as they should be.  Battery life is about 3.5 hours with the default 6-Cell.


The machine comes with 32-bit Vista Home Premium on it, which runs the best I've ever seen Vista run on a system-- at least right now.  The parents have taken a liking to it unfortunately, so I'm going to take about 20GB of their drive and try to win them back over with Ubuntu and Compiz.  Only problem I can foresee is the touch controls not functioning, which is a slight problem since they have some bound to regularly used items (mute, etc.).

### Post by conundrumx on 2008-08-13
> **Ub1476 said:**
> I heard it wasn't worth purchasing anyway. If it did any good, it was very little, and sometimes it made things worse.

Intel's Turbo Memory (Robson, whatever you'd like to call it) does increase the read speed of your hard drive, and to a lesser extent your battery life.  It slows down hibernate and standby.

When I bought my T61p it was something like 30 bucks for a gig, and I figured the worst thing that could happen is I would lose 30 dollars.  It doesn't seem like there is any *nix support coming, so I'm out 30 bucks.

Basically, it was a great idea implemented poorly and restricted to a single OS.

### Post by Miguel on 2008-08-13
> **legolas_w said:**
> In Lenovo site I can not find anything about T61P or T61i... there are only T61 in 14 and 15 inches with integrated and discrete graphic card. can you please explain about different of a T61p with T61?

Thanks

The main difference is/used to be the use of Workstation class graphics in the p version. More concretely, nVidia Quadro or ATi FireGL instead of GeForce or Radeon. Although the chips are almost identical to their desktop counterparts, different BIOS and driver optimisations make these work many, many times faster in CAD programs such as Catia, ProEngineer and similar stuff I haven't heard of.

### Post by archer6 on 2008-08-13
> **legolas_w said:**
> In Lenovo site I can not find anything about T61P or T61i... there are only T61 in 14 and 15 inches with integrated and discrete graphic card. can you please explain about different of a T61p with T61?

Lenovo is in a period of transition with new models currently being put up on their web site. 

Until just recently the "T" series were comprised of the regular models, and then the "workstation" class machines which were designated with the "p" suffix at the end of the model designation. 

I have both T60 and T60p models. The T Series ThinkPads are high-end notebooks in every way, while the 'p' in the name identifies this T Series as a mobile workstation model. As such, this machine is equipped with an ATI Mobility FireGL V5200 graphics card. Although the 3D performance on the FireGL V5200 will be pretty good, that's only half the story. The point of a workstation graphics chipset is that it is certified for a number of heavy duty applications such as Autocad, 3D Studio Max and SoftImage. Obviously a notebook computer is unlikely to be as powerful as a full-on desktop graphics workstation, but if you find yourself on the road regularly and want to be able to either work, or demonstrate projects to clients, a mobile workstation can be a real bonus. In addition the p models offered a hi-res 1600x1200 UXGA IPS Flexview display. This is an exemplary display which many rate as the finest ever offered on a laptop. 

Going forward the "new" workstation class machines that Lenovo offer are designated "W" on the web site. The main omission I see is the lack of a UXGA display being offered. However that is not to say that they may not in the future. See the link below "Lenovo W series" this will take to you their current workstation lineup. 

[Lenovo W series]("http://shop.lenovo.com/SEUILibrary/controller/e/web/LenovoPortal/en_US/catalog.workflow:category.details?current-catalog-id=12F0696583E04D86B9B79B0FEC01C087&current-category-id=F2A3EC7C45634AE8AB0F26CCAC867854")

Hopefully this answers your questions. 

Cheers ..... ;)

---

### Post by KWM987 on 2008-08-13
> **50words said:**
> GIMP and Blender work fine. It's video editing that I cannot do.

One possibility is that Avidemux, PiTiVi, et al. just don't work that well. I can convert videos to friendlier formats with Avidemux (usually after a few tries), but every video editor I have tried crashes when I try to edit the video.

I cannot use Cheese, either, to capture video. Sometimes it works, but the preview never does, and I often have to restart Cheese a few times to see what I recorded.

I don't like Cinelerra, but it does not work any better for me, anyway.

Or, it could be related to my ATI graphics card, but everything else graphics-related, including Compiz, works flawlessly.

It is bad enough that I am considering re-installing Windows XP or even getting a copy of Vista.

PiTiVi is still in pretty early development, so it's not too surprising that you may have problems with it.  It worked for me without a need to configure anything, I regularly use it to edit out commercials from recorded shows, and encode to more universal formats.  

You probably have already tried it, but if you haven't maybe Kino will work for you, since I know it supports DV capture, and is also an editor.  

Like you said, it can also be a hardware/horsepower issue; video  editing requires a good bit of power and RAM, I was forced to edit a 20 minute movie on one of on my previous MacMini's for a school project (1.42Ghz PPC, 1 GB RAM, discrete ATI graphics), even using a commercial program with iMovie HD I had issues similar to what you described, with things not displaying, refreshing, or just going so slow that the program eventually crashes, it took 16 hours for that one clip, and I knew it was directly related to power and resources.

I would not recommend windows for this type of work at all, I VERY briefly used 64-bit Vista Ultimate on my T61 to see if I could do this type of work on my system, and then get an easy connection to my Xbox 360 through WMP 11 or Vista Media Center.  Not only was it like running on molasses, but it decided it knew better than me on how to configure itself for this type of task and broke most of its power management and process handling rendering it mostly unusable.  After that I moved the system to wholly Ubuntu and have not had any problem getting it to work the way I want.  One thing to keep in mind is usually anything on Linux that isn't working can be made to work in some way, it's just how patient you want to be with it, but even I must admit I've run into moments where I thought converting my system into a boat anchor was a good idea.

I'm glad it was useful, they are a good little machine, I would buy one again.

### Post by Miguel on 2008-08-14
> **legolas_w said:**
> Thank you for reply.
I can not find them in Lenovo site, should I look somewhere else for them?

Thanks

As someone said before, Lenovo is pushing the Thinkpad line through a renewal path. Thus, the **new** Workstation-class laptops won't be called T61p or T43p or whatever, but W400 (14.1 inch), or W500 (15.4 inch) or W700 (17 inch, what a surprise). They will most likely change the last numerals with newer models, but the "centuries" are likely to stand for a while for signaling screen size. Have a look [here](http://shop.lenovo.com/SEUILibrary/controller/e/na/LenovoPortal/en_US/catalog.workflow:expandcategory?current-catalog-id=12F0696583E04D86B9B79B0FEC01C087&current-category-id=8FA114A7D9FF4F38AE8E19B36EC665A7) to see Lenovo's explanation.

### Post by archer6 on 2008-08-14
> **legolas_w said:**
> Do you use external mouse and keyboard with your Thinkpad?

I have always enjoyed the very accurate ThinkPad TrackPoint. 

All new ThinkPads come with a choice of three differently shaped Red Rubber Caps, so that one has a choice of shapes. If you have a used ThinkPad the caps are available on the Lenovo web site. 

The key is to go into the preferences and play around with the settings for travel, amount of effort required to move it, etc. Once that is done you will find that this type of pointing device is far superior to any other choices. Your finger falls right in place while typing and it's super fast. For over a decade I've enjoyed this type of pointing device. Also it's far superior to the "copy cats" as implemented on some Dell's etc. 

Nothing beats the ThinkPad TrackPoint.

### Post by archer6 on 2008-09-07
> **Ub1476 said:**
> Woho, I'll be getting my Thinkpad X200 with the 64GB SSD on Saturday! 

I want to see how Ubuntu does it). I wonder if you know which filesystem I should use and how I disable SWAP (I need to do that right)? Also, any other "special for Thinkpads" tips?

Hows Linux/Ubuntu with battery life on Thinkpads? I hope that it wont be worse than Windows (XP).. Also any tips for keeping the battery life 100%? After it's charged, should I drain it completely so it just gets "used" a bit, then refrain from draining completely (below 10%, 5%?) later?

Congratulations on your new ThinkPad X200!

My suggestion would be to install Ubuntu 8.04, I've had nothing but perfect results on my ThinkPads. It will setup the proper file system automatically during the install. 
Battery: I would highly encourage you to charge it overnight the first evening you receive it. My experience is that Ubuntu, is very easy on battery usage. I routinely get about 30% better battery run times on Ubuntu 8.04. (versus XP Pro SP3) After your first "full charge" its a good idea to run it until the computer shuts down. Fully draining the battery. Then charge it fully and repeat this full recharge / discharge cycle just three times. After that you can use the battery without it requiring any special attention. My personal routine is to run the battery down to 15% then recharge it. Also if you tend to be plugged in a lot when in the office, at home or wherever, no worries as ThinkPad Batteries are very durable. Just do not leave your laptop in a Hot Car. There is no faster way to kill a battery. 

Enjoy your new ThinkPad....Cheers....:)

---

### Post by archer6 on 2008-09-08
> **Ub1476 said:**
> Excellent, I recieved it yesterday. It's probably the best computer I have ever seen. :)

According to the Thinkvantage (amazing software, wish it was for Linux), my battery has done 2 cycles. Personally I have only done it once. It tend to be plugged in a lot at home, but at school the battery will be used for 6 hoursn +/-. Guess that's enough exercise for it?
Congratulations! I knew you would love your new ThinkPad, and it only gets better as you use it and find all the little details that add up to one terrific experience. 

With all due modesty, I'm very knowledgeable about ThinkPads as:

A) I've used them everyday for a decade, (as my main computer), have owned over 45 new ones myself, and deploy 325 in my company, all of which have been replaced with new models on an 18month timeline, therefore I've seen well over a few thousand. Call me an addict if you must....:) but after it's all said and done, I know exactly what a ThinkPad benefits from, in terms of being optimised for everyting from max batt life to fast, terrific , reliable performance. 

B) As a super geek with a dual masters from MIT in Electrical Engineering, & Computer Science, while I still have lots to learn, I do know battery technologies and stay updated. While a bit of a pain to do, 3 (cycles of charge / discharge)times is the charm, even with the newest of batteries. If you don't care how long your battery lasts, just use it. However it you want to get amazing life that will blow away the "experts" just do the 3 Cycle protocol and you will not be buying a battery for a long time.
