### Post by Dragonbite on 2008-09-12
This sucks.. Lenovo confirms ditching Linux.
> 
[http://www.desktoplinux.com/news/NS8639308872.html?kc=rss]("http://www.desktoplinux.com/news/NS8639308872.html?kc=rss")
A day after it denied ditching pre-installed desktop Linux, PC giant Lenovo has confirmed to Stephen Vaughan-Nichols of Practical Technology that "customers will no longer be able to order Lenovo ThinkPads and ThinkCentres with pre-installed Linux via the lenovo.com website."

I can understand their decision (mentioned in the link above) though. Lenovo has so much work supporting Windows that they don't have time to learn how to support anything else!

---

### Post by Dragonbite on 2008-09-12
WAIT...

"Lenovo Launching Linux Netbooks"

> [http://www.thevarguy.com/2008/09/11/lenovo-launching-linux-netbooks/]("http://www.thevarguy.com/2008/09/11/lenovo-launching-linux-netbooks/")
Lenovo has faced some criticism from Linux folks in recent days, amid reports that the company was abandoning desktop Linux preloads. In an email to The VAR Guy, Di Valerio clarified Lenovo’s strategy:

    [QUOTE]Our commitment to Linux has not changed. What’s changed is that customers will no longer be able to order Lenovo ThinkPads and ThinkCentres with pre-installed Linux via the lenovo.com website. We are still certifying Linux pre-loads on ThinkPads and ThinkCentres, but most of those customers typically order either through their Lenovo Sales team or Lenovo Business Partner.
...
    In addition, we will be delivering new Linux offerings on our new Lenovo servers, and Ideapad Netbook education models, scheduled for release in September and October respectively.

...

Canonical, the company behind Ubuntu Linux, predicted that Netbooks running Ubuntu would be available in all major U.S. computer retail stores in late 2008 or 2009.
[/QUOTE]

They are really starting to confuse me!

### Post by archer6 on 2008-09-12
> **71CH said:**
> This is a bit OT but the plastic palm-rest thingy around my trackpad is a bit loose.  Any suggestions?  Thanks.

> **zmjjmz said:**
> Actually, it's the same on my Mom's.

What you two are experiencing is normal and repairable. Since the wrist rest was changed to two pieces, (upper and lower) this has been an issue. All one must do is remove the lower palm rest, and refit it carefully so as not to have the looseness you are noticing now. It simply clips into place and carefully refitted it makes the noise and movement go away. I have done this successfully on my T60 / Z60m models as they too have the same design. Now a year after the repairs they are still quiet, free of movement, and fit really well. Problem solved. 

Here's the fix for each of you. Please ignore that it says for R60 as it works for 61 series as well. This link below includes a drawing of the procedure along with a text explanation on how to remove and replace the palm rest. In addition, it includes a short movie to show how to do the procedure. 

[ThinkPad Palm Rest Movie]("http://www-307.ibm.com/pc/support/site.wss/document.do?lndocid=MIGR-64362")

Cheers
ThinkPads.... is there anything else?

### Post by archer6 on 2008-09-16
> **whyette said:**
> They won't be offering Linux pre-installed when ordering online. I believe you can still order them by calling, or through an actual store.
Unfortunately your info is incorrect. 
There are no "actual stores", for a period of time they tried selling the bottom of the line R models through Office Depot, but that program flopped. There may be some still out there but as they are sold they are not being replaced. IBM, then Lenovo has never sold (other than the one time I just cited which was a limited test) through the retail store channels. 
As far as calling in is concerned, the same thing applies, they are _not_ preloading Linux for retail customers. There is one exception I'm aware of as I'm a large scale business owner. That exception is for very large orders, and even that program has been "temporarily suspended". Thus it's the typical IBM/Lenovo approach to Linux. On and off.

### Post by archer6 on 2008-09-17

Here is the link to [Parts Lookup]("http://www-307.ibm.com/pc/support/site.wss/product.do?template=%2Fpartslookup%2FpartsLookup.vm&sitestyle=lenovo") for ThinkPads
Simply enter your model type & serial numbers, it will then return a specific list of all components that are in your ThinkPad. This will help you research any issues you are having.

### Post by yosumi on 2008-09-17
R51:
1) Has the best mouse buttons. The only laptop that does not have those annoying click sound buttons.
2) No "start" button in keyboard. (Unfortunately newer thinkpads have it) Nice big keyboard buttons.
3) Linux friendly.
4) Looks professional.
5) Thinklight. (I didn't notice the hole on top was actually a light bulb until recently.)
6) Magnesium alloy casing. Unbreakable hinges.

I bought this R51 a year ago for $500. Specs: 1.6Ghz Centrino 2MB L2 cache. 40GB IDE hard disk. Radeon 9000. (Can't use full effects) 

Although more expensive than mainstream laptops in terms of specs, thinkpad's quality is worth every penny.

It's just that none of them have the terrific, massive web site like Lenovo / IBM that will provide one with everything you could possibly want in the way of support files, drivers, firmware updates, BIOS updates, you name it, the site has it. When you call their 24/7 tech support line you get an English Speaking person in Atlanta Georgia, who is trained and knows how to assist you in a very quick and accurate fashion. 

From a standpoint of Linux, ThinkPads in my personal experience are simply the best. Just great performance!

### Post by archer6 on 2008-09-23
> **fsando said:**
> I'm going to buy a lenovo next time and as far as I can tell the only obstacle is that their screens are not too bright. Is that a correct assessment?> **Dragonbite said:**
> I'm interested in that.  I want whatever laptop I have be good for brightness and colors for digital pictures and the like.
Yes it is correct to say that ThinkPads have lower levels of screen brightness. That said, let me pose this question, just how important is it really? I do a tremendous amount of photography work via my previous T models (T30,40,42,43) and now T60. Having used ThinkPads for so long I find the extra brightness on my 2 mo old MacBook Pro with LED backlight _too_ bright. And that's not a criticism, just an observation. What purpose does it serve? Impressive at first glance... yes. But truly needed? In my humble opinion no. For me it's the quality of the lighting, the color depth, contrast, and staturation that matter. This is an area that ThinkPads have excelled in for some time. Especially those models like my T60 and T60p with the IPS Flexview technology. Widely regarded as the finest in laptop displays regardless of nits. These displays are a photographers dream. IPS is the Flexview LCD displays used on select IBM/Lenovo models use the IPS (In-Plane Switching) technology, which provide wide viewing angles (the display can literally be seen from all angles with no color distortions and minimal loss of contrast), better contrast (making the whites brighter and the blacks darker) and richer colors overall than the regular laptop screens. They also incorporae a second technology the FFS (Fringe Field Switching) technology is patented by BOE-Hydis LCD manufacturing company and extends conventional IPS technology by offering even wider viewing angles (full 180 degrees are claimed) and better transmittance.

---

> **archer6 said:**

That's good to know. I know brightness isn't the only quality which is why I put in that bit about digital images, because color depth and such are as or more important to brightness but I haven't gotten into it to such a degree to have discern the differences or who is better at what.

### Post by dfumento on 2008-09-25
I have an X61s Ultralight 1.8 GHz, 4 GB, 200 GB 7200 running Windows Server 2008 32-bit.  I've installed Sun xVM VirtualBox and installed Ubuntu with 10 GB disk, 512 MB RAM.  It works really well and I'm really pleased.  The only thing to watch out for is to ensure your system is set up for virtual processing in the BIOS and that the VT setting is set for your virtual machine in VirtualBox.  If this option is not set, your install will hang.

Performance is good.

I'm getting an X200s when they become available with the 1440x900 screen.  Other models of X200s and X200 have 1280x800 screens compared to 1024x768 of previous "X" models.

Most people will find that the X200(s) will suit their needs well and that they no longer need a larger laptop.

I rarely need the optical drive and I have an very portable USB 2.0 external optical drive when I need it.

check out forum.thinkpads.com

### Post by archer6 on 2008-09-25
> **fsando said:**
> I'm doing a lot of custom computational work which forces me in front of the computer for many hours most days, so the the joy of a great keyboard and a great screen would really really make my day (days as a matter of fact).
Thanks for you replies - they're helping a lot in my decision to buy (probably) a thinkpad.
Now that I know more about your usage patterns, I can see why it would indeed be an advantage to you. Just another comment for what it's worth, (and I'm not being critical of Apple) but largely because most people do not have the luxury I do of having owned so many Laptops of all brands, they have no idea just how good a ThinkPad keyboard is. In addition, while the MacBook Pro is generally perceived as having a great keyboard, I can tell you that it's not. It's clearly second best. The difference is the "keystroke depth". 2.5mm is the vertical travel of the keys on ThinkPad T series for example. Whereas the keystroke depth, or travel on a MBP is only 1.9mm (Sony VAIO's are 1.8mm, on average Dells are 1.9mm) this is a huge difference if one is a writer, or does other work in which one uses the keyboard extensively. That's just another reason why I prefer ThinkPads far beyond any other laptop. Yes, I own and enjoy a new MBP as I've always enjoyed PowerBooks and the like, however if I was limited to just one laptop, its ThinkPad hands down, no contest, no hesitation, just

### Post by archer6 on 2008-09-29
> **fsando said:**
> I have a couple of questions

What about the new w500? Is it good for linux? I like it because it comes with faster cpu, and on top of that it's 'downgradeble' to xp by default ;)

Secondly, some of you (especially archer) seem to be using several laptops, how do you synchronize between computers?
I really like the new W500, a very good friend of mine has had his for about two weeks. He sold his T60p and replaced it with the W500 because in his case he's a fan of widescreen. We got together last weekend and I was able to put it through it's paces and I highly endorse this model. 

Here's my suggestion for you:
1) I would buy the most loaded W500 I could afford. Get a 160GB or larger hard drive. Make sure you order it with the XP downgrade. 

2) I would purchase Ubuntu 8.04.1 already burned to a DVD, as burning ISO images to disc does not always result in a good clean image. (it depends on the quality and consistency of your burner, but that's a long story) This is what I did and the cost is less than the time you would spend doing it yourself. Plus the link I am including offers it on DVD which gives you more free software than if you get the CD. I do not work for this company, just a very happy customer. They process the order quickly and ship it, I got mine in about 4 days from: [OSDisc.com]("http://www.osdisc.com/cgi-bin/view.cgi/products/linux/ubuntu/i386.html")Cost is $5.95

3) I would divide the W500 hard drive into two partitions, one for Windows, and one for Ubuntu. The utility that comes with the Live DVD makes this super easy. It will even split the disc in half automatically, so don't have to do anything, just let it run. The entire process of partitioning and installing is just over an hour. It's so easy you will be shocked. 

4) Now you have the best of both worlds. Windows to rely on an run as you normally do, then Ubuntu to boot to and learn, until you are comfortable enough to run it all the time, or whatever you decide is the best usage for you. In the event you don't like it, this is a very OS to remove. However that said I'm sure you will really enjoy it and most probably use it far more than you anticipated. I know that as a first time linux user, I immediately became so impressed with it, I put it on all my laptops. I have just two apps that I must run under XP, so I will run windows a while longer until I have time to find suitable replacements for these apps. 

Finally, as far as keeping my laptops in sync, I'm a really picky about this, and they are exactly the same so at any time I can grab one and go. I use a USB external Hard Drive. The model is "Passport" made by Western Digital. This is another product I highly endorse. I have a couple (it's my OCD tendencies about always being backed up, in sync, and the convenience of having one at home and one in my laptop case) The first Passport I purchased two years ago when they first came out, and it's still working perfectly. They are very small, light, and extremely durable. I am providing a link to Western Digital for your reference. If you decide to buy one go to newegg.com as they have the best prices. Here is the link to WD: [WD Passport]("http://www.wdc.com/en/products/index.asp?Cat=9")

### Post by fsando on 2008-09-29
> **50words said:**
> I use Unison to sync up my laptop with my office server. I find it very convenient and easy to use. What was the problem you were having?

I'm thinking that I may have misunderstood how Unison works but this is what I have experienced.

I know this may sound a bit odd but I have about 15Gb that I need to have access to always and that needs to be up-to-date all the time. Most of it never or rarely changes. So I'd like to make a sync-job that only looked into the few folders I know changes, perhaps two jobs, the "frequent changes job" and the "infrequent changes job". One problem is that the folders that change are scattered through out this 15Gb.

How do I make an elaborate sync-job where I pick and choose files and folders in Unison? 
To me it seems a job essentially means pointing Unison to a local folder and a remote folder, then Unison runs through both folders and presents a list of possible differences and lets you decide what to do. As far as I can tell the list can't be sorted and, you can only choose one file at a time which make it quite cumbersome.
The only way I can see how to make a more elaborate job is by going through the list and 'ignoring' individual files or folders.

Imagine that process for 15Gb (I actually went through it to test the app), and then think about the time it took to go through all that data in the first place (both in the local and the remote).

That's why Unison doesn't feel so convenient in my situation.

---

### Post by 50words on 2008-09-29
For a job like that, I would probably use the Unison command line. Like you say, the Unison GUI is fairly simplified.

However, if the other folders are not changing, then there is no harm in pointing Unison to the whole directory. It will only sync files that change, after all. I use it to sync about 20GB over the web.

### Post by archer6 on 2008-09-30
> **fsando said:**
> Thanks for your answer.
Apparently only this one is available in my country (it's in Danish but the specs are pretty much international anyway)

[http://edbpriser.dk/Products/Listprices.asp?ID=473291&Specs=true](http://edbpriser.dk/Products/Listprices.asp?ID=473291&Specs=true)

It says the screen is "TFT active matrix", I'm not sure what exactly that means. The price tag is 'only' around 3000 $

Another thing is that it says that max memory is 3Gb. I'm not sure if this is correct or if it has to do with 32bit Vista limitations. All the W models on lenovo's site are announced to have max=4Gb. 

I've looked around and my impression is that this is a custom build for the Danish market. All shops have the exact same model (another model is mentioned but none has it in stock)

I will review the link you sent carefully and I have sent you a PM. "TFT" stands for Think Film Transistor" it's the basis for all common LCD displays today. Then there are variations of that technology such as IPS and others. Active Matrix & Passive Matrix are two (fairly old) technologies & standards which result in displays that look quite different. Passive Matrix was the first LCD color display type to be created many years ago, it produces a color display that is rather washed out looking, and not very clear in terms of font definition etc. Then came Active Matrix which was & is very clear. It became the standard replacing Passive Matrix. 

As far as memory limit of 3GB that has to do with not only the limits of 32bit Windows addressable memory, but a bit of hardware config issues as well. 

Cheers

---

### Post by Dragonbite on 2008-10-13
I had my Dell laptop and my Thinkpad T60 (61?) side-by-side and looked at the screen differences.

The Dell was definitely brighter but to have the color accurate I have to look at it "just right". Depending on the angle there is a greater chance to the color being washed out if you aren't looking at it right than the Thinkpad.

The Thinkpad was not-so-bright but was visible from a wider angle which made it a lot easier on the eyes. This makes the color and image is more consistent across the entire screen.

I have to admit, though, I have not fooled around with the contrast or gamma on either machines.

### Post by archer6 on 2008-10-27

In the days that have elapsed since I began to think about your interest in this model I have given it much thought, as I have a long history of experience with ThinkPads. I feel very confident that you are making a great decision and that you will be very happy with the result. The combination of architecture and  a 32 bit OS, allows just 3GB to be addressable. Take note that it's what I use in my T-60 ThinkPad revealed in my signature line.  However that said, as a software engineer, I can tell you that there is a lot of dialog and argument around this. So for sake of conversation let's set aside the hard data. Instead let's talk about real world performance. I find with 3GB I have far more ram than I need in 80% of instances. The 20% of the time that I require more, it's only because I'm running super resource intensive 3D design, or scientific apps that one would only use if doing the type of work I do. Even the power users I know find that they never stress their ThinkPad anywhere near the level that I must operate at. Therefore I'm completely certain that at 3GB you would not only have plenty of ram, but be well future proofed as well. ### Post by archer6 on 2008-10-27
> **50words said:**
> I guess I don't care for an objective judgment of battery performance. I just want to know how long I can use my laptop before I have to go in search of an outlet.
There are far too many variables such as: hardware configuration, apps running, power management settings, LCD brightness setting, wifi on or off, other connected peripherals, settings for time outs, such as dim display, or blank display, shut down hard drive after X amount of idle time. et.al. Therefore there is no way to judge how a given setup will work for one user as opposed to the other.  

> **50words said:**
> If the Apple hardware is more efficient, then perhaps it is not really the "exact same hardware" as many like to say. Maybe there is a good reason it costs more.
The concept, or belief that Apple hardware is more efficient is a Myth, perpetuated by the ever masterful salesman and deceptive marketing genius of Steve Jobs. This man is brilliant. The very best I've witnessed. I have purchased over a dozen Apple laptops in a decade, and compared them side by side with my ThinkPads that were as identically configured as possible. While not by any stretch of the imagination, a perfect scientific testing environment. I have benchmarked the various machines which revealed great similarities. That said, I've never made an outright statement that one is far superior over the other as that is largely a matter of personal preference. It is their differences that I celebrate. Their individual strengths that I enjoy. And the very reason I own both. If I have one complaint and I do, it's just that of all the various brands and models of laptops I own, none of those companies, insist on pushing their agenda on others as Apple does. None of them belittle the competition. None of them make the ridiculous claims that Apple does. None of them rip off the customers with outrageous pricing as Apple does. If they did they would be as cash rich as Apple is this very moment. 
Setting that aside, I find Apple designs stellar, I find their packaging world class. I find the overall experience quite nice. But at the end of the day as I become more experienced with Ubuntu, I find the user experience far superior to any other OS I use. And I use all three, Windows, Mac, and Linux.

Great Stuff! A wonderful trip down memory lane, much of which I can certainly relate to. In my case going back to the very first "notebook" class computer as created by Apple in 1991, and my first laptop, the **_[PowerBook 140]("http://www.apple-history.com/")_**. Now here was a fun machine. A huge trackball protruding up from the palm rest was the very best, most fun and easy to use pointing device I've experienced. But alas, after a short time, and too many users spilling their coffee, sodas, pizza crumbs and what not into that beauty, it became a liability. So Apple, and a small unknown vendor created the touch pad, to replace the track ball. Personally I found the TrackBall very easy to keep clean, and used mine for three years problem free. Next up was a **Toshiba** with a color display, built in CD rom, and the first TrackStick with a bright green rubber cap. Apparently not protected by patent, IBM, created the **ThinkPad** with it's own TrackPoint which was, for me, the next best thing to the trackball. Thus began my ongoing use of both ThinkPads (for work) and PowerBooks (for play).

Cheers...:)

---

### Post by archer6 on 2008-10-27
> **50words said:**
> archer6, are you saying ThinkPads do get as-good or better battery life then MacBooks? Because I have never seen it.

Here is the only benchmark I care about: if I am browsing the web and using OpenOffice.org, which one will last longer? Sitting next to my wife on the couch, doing those things, her MacBook always goes at least an hour longer than my ThinkPad, often more.
I can only speak from personal experience. And for clarification, when it comes to testing and evaluation of the various laptops, I remain very brand neutral. I do indeed set my opinions aside and just test and compare. It's what I enjoy doing, thus the reason I have such a huge inventory of laptops [not bragging, more of a confession of my passion..:)] In my most recent test less than 3 months ago, being the obsessive tester I am, I compared my six mo. old Black MacBook (OS X Leopard) to a similarly equipped 14" ThinkPad T61 (Ubuntu 8.04). Ironically it was a "sitting on the couch test" on a Sunday while watching a couple of Formula One races I had Tivo'd. Wifi on, OpenOffice on both laptops, as I was reviewing reports, and browsing the web via Firefox 3.0.2. The results were eerily similar. In this particular test, the MacBook lasted 17 minutes longer. An amount of time that for me is relatively insignificant. The deal breaker, or decision maker for me when talking run time comes in 30 min intervals. At a mere 17 minutes it's too close to make a difference to me. "Your mileage may vary"...:)

I hope this helps,
Cheers

### Post by archer6 on 2008-10-31
> **50words said:**
> I thought I might try sending it in to get a few little things fixed that bother me, like the loose power connecter and the bit of plastic that broke off the side near the DVD drive.
I would highly encourage you to do just that, call up the tech support department (yes I know your out of warranty) and explain to them that you have several items that need replacement. I'm not sure if they still have the same program as last year but here is my story: I have a T42 that I was given. Cosmetically fresh, but had a bad hard drive, a cracked display, and other numerous issues. When it was first given to me I was going to use it for parts until I learned about the unofficial "refurbishment" program they have. As I began to list off what it needed the very courteous and helpful tech support person offered to write it up for something that was a known fault (bad memory slots), and suggested that if I simply included a list in the box I returned it in, they would take care of the other items such as replacing the keyboard, LCD, trackpoint, touchpad, new hard drive, optical drive, and mouse buttons etc. Long story short, refurbished for $375. Now I have a fresh T42 that just happens to be my second favorite (after my T60) of the T series I've had, which is all of them.

### Post by fsando on 2008-11-06
> **archer6 said:**
> Excellent ! Keep us posted

Cheers
archer

Now I've got it!

BUT, but, but:

I've tried to install Intrepid without success :(
I've tried with 32bit desktop and 64bit desktop and my attempts so far have all ended at the command prompt:

'ubuntu@ubuntu:~$'

Trying to run 'gdm' tells me it's already running so it's as if a final step isn't run.

I've seen a few sporadic reports of what I think is the same issue with inconclusive responses.

I'm a little stumped right now.

EDIT:
Turns out the solution is really easy.
You have to set the 'Graphics device = discrete graphics' in BIOS as explained [here]("http://art.ubuntuforums.org/showpost.php?t=969504").

Now preparing install from livecd :)

Or try:
Online Resellers in Sweden, [http://www.hitachigst.com/portal/site/en/template.PAGE/buy/?country_code=se&where_to_buy=reseller_list](http://www.hitachigst.com/portal/site/en/template.PAGE/buy/?country_code=se&where_to_buy=reseller_list)

[INDENT]Section "Device"

### Post by Miguel on 2008-11-12
I'm on the verge of buying a T400, and I really mean "on the verge", since it's very likely I'm buying it on friday. It pisses me off though that the only models with stock in spain don't have the LED-backlit screen. Ordering a LED-backlit one will take one month and cost me in excess of $500. Can anybody tell me whether I'll miss a lot with a conventional CCFL screen?

---

### Post by archer6 on 2008-11-12
> **Miguel said:**
> I'm on the verge of buying a T400, and I really mean "on the verge", since it's very likely I'm buying it on friday. It pisses me off though that the only models with stock in spain don't have the LED-backlit screen. Ordering a LED-backlit one will take one month and cost me in excess of $500. Can anybody tell me whether I'll miss a lot with a conventional CCFL screen?
I've been upgrading to a new T-series ThinkPad at every product cycle as new models are introduced, for the last 8 years, with just one exception being last year as they discontinued the 1600x1200 IPS Flexview 4x3 format displays which are optimum for my scientific work. I use my laptop as my main work machine everyday. I've been perfectly happy with CCFL. 

A fellow engineer friend of mine works in the field of LED back lighting for laptop displays. He advises me that there is a distinct advantage to _avoiding_ LED lighting. Apparently that as soon as month number 3, they begin to slowly dim, losing their brightness gradually over a three year period. So if you only plan on keeping it 12 to 18 months the negatives are minor, however if you plan on owning it longer he highly suggests CCFL. I know that I'm approaching year three with my T60 ThinkPad with a 15" IPS Flexview display and it's every bit as bright and clear today as it was on day one. I gave my 4 year old T43 to my son for college, and it's every bit as good as my 2 year old T60p, all of these are CCFL, so I'm sure you will be happy without the LED option. 

Hope this helps you,
Cheers

---

> Hi
Are you saying that MacBook pros are equipped with less durable screens than Lenovo? Ie. don't buy an MBP if you intend to keep it for more than a couple of years?
First a brief bit of background. I have dual masters degrees from MIT in the fields of electrical engineering and computer science. Not boasting, but simply mentioned it as a point of reference in terms of my knowledge, experience has been 10 years of using laptops as my main computer. During this decade I've used ThinkPads as my primary computer for work (as I deploy 200+ ThinkPads in the company I founded 15years ago). For personal usage I use macs. I've owned every PowerBook beginning with the 140 which was the one of the first three (100/140/170) PowerBooks made my Apple. Each year I've upgraded both my PowerBooks, now MacBook Pros, as well as my personal ThinkPads. So again, with all due modesty I only share this as a point of reference. 

On Topic regarding back lighting I'm merely sharing engineering data in my prior post which addresses the statistical data regarding the different types of back lighting. Now as far as an LED back light MacBook Pro, I will find out personally as I purchased one the day they were released. The attraction for me is the brighter even lighting. However that said, ThinkPads have always had very evenly lit displays on their more expensive models, and the lesser machines like the R models are still excellent. And as far as illumination level I've always, and continue to find them very good. Just not super bright. Back to our topic of longevity, while every indication, and the supporting data reveals the LED to dim beginning from day one in a very linear and gradual way. Despite laboratory testing, the exact light level at the end of a three year period has not been perfectly identified. However all indicators are that there will indeed be a diminished light level. However that said one must remember that they begin being brighter than say the comparable ThinkPad. So as we all know there is always a certain difference between hard data and real world experience. In this case the level of brightness that is chosen by the user as he/she sets up the machine to their preferences is also an influencing factor. Thus so many variables come into play that only time will tell. Bottom line? Speaking only for myself, while I really enjoy the LED back light option, I have years of experience, thousands and thousands of hours in front of both ThinkPads and PowerBooks / MBP that have been just fine with CCFL. Therefore I was simply assuring the OP who mentioned that the option for LED meant a $500, that I did not think they would neither suffer without it, nor do I think it's worth the $500 premium and that's speaking purely from a practical engineers viewpoint. Finally for whatever it's worth. I find ThinkPads as my number one favorite, with the Mac laptops a close second. 

Hopefully this answers your question. 

Cheers...:)

### Post by archer6 on 2008-11-15
> **Miguel said:**
> Hi guys,

I've got some questions. I've read that the new Thinkpads (centrino2 generation, like the T400) are sold with 3 partitions already created. Apparently the hard drive is partitioned into the well-known C:\ for Vista, and then S:\ for boot stuff and Q:\ for the recovery stuff.

Are these primary partitions? Can they be safely removed? If I am paying for Vista, I'd like to keep the chance of booting into it. I would also like to create a primary swap partition, although that seems unlikely if all three partitions are primary.
This may indeed be unique to Lenovo's choice when preloading them with Vista. I do not have a Vista machine. I ordered my T400 with XP which I greatly prefer. It's what I have my company, and my personal machines centered on . My T400 came with the customary (for Lenovo) two partitions. C:\ which is where XP Pro resides as does any data you store. Then there is a "hidden" partition which cannot be seen". (unless of course you are using a partitioning utility). The "hidden" partition is where the oem drive image is stored, it is accessible via the Blue ThinkVantage key located at the top left of the keyboard. If the event of a catastrophic crash, one simply shuts down. Then reboots while holding down this key and it then gives you the option to reload the OS, which will wipe the drive, reload everything, and you then have a system that is fresh, just as though you were unpacking it for the very first time and setting it up. The entire process takes about an hour, has very little "bloatware" which once up and running I can remove in less than 10 minutes. At which point we are good to go. A very handy option, unless you need every last MB of space in which case some users choose to delete it. 

Hope this helps you.

Cheers...

### Post by fsando on 2008-11-16
> **Miguel said:**
> Hi guys,

I've got some questions. I've read that the new Thinkpads (centrino2 generation, like the T400) are sold with 3 partitions already created. Apparently the hard drive is partitioned into the well-known C:\ for Vista, and then S:\ for boot stuff and Q:\ for the recovery stuff.

Are these primary partitions? Can they be safely removed? If I am paying for Vista, I'd like to keep the chance of booting into it. I would also like to create a primary swap partition, although that seems unlikely if all three partitions are primary.

(EDIT: My laptop is T500 not T400 but I suppose it's the same)

That's true, they are all three primary partitions.
I made a backup image with partimage of all three to an external hd.

What I did was the following
I connected an external drive, booted from the livecd, then I first resized the partitions to as small as possible (for one of the drives it took more than a hour perhaps two hours, I don't remember clearly).
After that I installed partimage through synaptic (I think you have to enable 'community-maintained' repository).

Then with partimage I made a complete image of all three partitions. I deleted all and installed Ubuntu. Then later I deleted everything again, made a new partition and copied the vista partition back - then did the XP 'downgrade'. After that I installed Ubuntu in the usual dual boot fashion.
That is, only the Vista partition is needed. At least in my case the xp-downgrade cd did what it should with only the vista partition present.

Of course after that I made an image of the finished xp-install as well.

For me the most important is that I can now get the initial partitions back should I want that.

CAUTION:
1) This was a very convoluted and time consuming process where I had to boot from livecd and install partimage every time I wanted to change partitions etc. (I did a lot of experiments). I believe the reason is that you need to unmount the drive which is impossible if you booted normally.
2) The reason I resized to as small as possible was that when you want to put the image back the partition must be at least as big as the original (the hole point was to save space for Linux).
3) It's probably a good idea to practice partimage a bit.

Now, that I'm satisfied with how my hd is partitioned I can use partimage from within Ubuntu with no problems.
I have
16 Gb for Xp
18 Gb for an ntfs drive shared between windows and Ubuntu
10 Gb for '/'
3 Gb for '/var'
100 Gb for '/home'
4 Gb for swap

---

### Post by archer6 on 2008-11-17
> **Miguel said:**
> OK, I'm writing this from my brand new T400!!!! I've got a few points I'd like to comment.

**1)** This one is minor. My model has Windows Vista Business downgraded to XP Pro. However, Vista CDs are nowhere to be found. I don't know if the recovery stuff when I boot will restore Vista or XP. I've read that Vista CD's are supposed to be shipped, and that Lenovo could mail them to me free of charge. I will research further into this. The license states this is a Vista Business machine. I wouldn't bother much, but hybrid graphics only work in Vista.

**2)** The right side of the palmrest (near the fingerprint reader) is a bit loose. Normally, I wouldn't bother much, but this being a thinkpad, I really expected somewhat better. Is there a way to tighten it so that it doesn't move as much when I put my hands there?

1a) When I received my T400 it took 3 or 4 more days for the Vista CD's to arrive, as they come from the Software Fulfillment Center. This is normal, they are not supposed to come in the box with the computer. The Rescue & Recovery partition will restore your computer to exactly the same way it came out of the box. In the event of a major crash, and it will not boot, or you simply want to wipe the drive and start fresh as it came out of the box, with the computer off, you simply push the power button, hold down the blue ThinkVantage button and a dialog box will come up and guide you through the process. I takes about 40 minutes. 

2a) The loose palmrest is really easy to take care of. I'm including a link that has a movie to demonstrate how it's done on a T60. Yes I know you have a T400, but that movie is not ready yet and I have both the T400 and the T60 and they are very similar, so this movie is close enough for you to get the idea and be able to take care of it yourself. It's really easy, takes about 15 minutes, and one Philips screwdriver to remove 3 or 4 screws on the bottom of the case. All that's wrong is when they slid the cover in place they missed one of the little clips. When you have it apart, it's really obvious. Also was a common issue on T60's as it's easy to miss if they do not go slow, and be sure to engage all tabs. 
[B][URL="http://www-307.ibm.com/pc/support/site.wss/document.do?lndocid=MIGR-62808"]
Palm Rest Movie [/URL][/B]

Enjoy your new T400, I'm loving mine!

### Post by Miguel on 2008-11-24
Hello everybody again,

I've just found out that there are a wealth of applications in order to expose the Thinkpad features to the OS. Even in linux! Honestly, I didn't expect to be able to modify charge thresholds or APS settings from Linux. But there are quite a few!

**tp-smapi**: Built within linux-ubuntu-modules since hardy. Is this safe to use on newer hardware? Any experience in T400 or similar? 

**hdaps**: Quite a few options here. There's the default module, plus the hdaps module contained in tp-smapi. ThinkWiki states that the hdaps inside tp-smapi in Ubuntu is called hdaps_ec. However, no such module exists. Is it OK? Does hdaps need a daemon in order to work properly?

Anyway, thanks again for your kind help.

### Post by Miguel on 2008-11-25
> **Ub1476 said:**
> Does anyone of you know what causes worse power-managment on Linux? Is it the intel graphic driver, or just the kernel in itself? Does the X200 just happen to be unfortunate?

I don't think the kernel is to blame, and neither should be the graphics driver (as long as you aren't running compiz). Current kernels are tickless (good) and have a very good scheduler (cool!) but not so good i/o scheduler for desktop usage. In any case, 2.6.27 seems to implement the needed bits in order to activate the most power-saving features.

In my system, a working Ubuntu (with firefox, claws-mail, a couple of terminals and rhythmbox running) uses about 12.4W. Mind you, this is the CCFL screen at medium brightness. I'd guess an idle system would need around 11~11.5W. WinXP Pro idles at about 9W. However, if I use WMP power consumption goes up to 11.4W and with MediaMonkey it rises up to 12.5W. My guess is that, with the exception of a few tricks, the real difference between XP and Linux here is HD power management*. I'll see what happens if I modprobe tp-smapi. 

* I guess HD power management is turned off by default because of [bug #59695](https://bugs.launchpad.net/ubuntu/+source/acpi-support/+bug/59695). Better draw an additional watt than kill the HD.

---

### Post by emergant on 2008-12-21
I'm mostly running a macbook pro now but I have just bought an old T41 off of Ebay and I'm impressed at how much laptop I got for how little outlay.

The keyboard is first rate.  The extra keys are nice too.

I've installed intrepid.  Had a great deal of grief with wireless which was solved as soon as I installed Wicd.

It will be mostly used for browsing and its 1.6 ghz pentium M, 512 mb and 40 gb hd are more than enough.

I suspect that the HD will die at some point but thats easily sorted.  I've seen some posts on the net about mobo/gpu problems? but this one seems fine.

The only wierdness I have is notes in dmesg regarding a corrupted ethernet eeprom.  I need to set a manual mac address.  There are notes on this on think wiki and I downloaded a script from Levenho but it didnt work on my hardware.  Not a big deal though.

Suspend/resume breaks wifi for some reason

I'm very happy though and if I ever need another non apple laptop I'll look at IBM

### Post by Miguel on 2008-12-21
> **emergant said:**
> 
I suspect that the HD will die at some point but thats easily sorted.  I've seen some posts on the net about mobo/gpu problems? but this one seems fine.


My old laptop belongs to the same generation of your T41. When your HD dies, you will have to replace it with an ATA one, since SATA HD's became available with later Pentium-M models (T43, I believe). In any case, get the bigger HD you can and don go lower than 5400rpm. Throughput is the real bottleneck of today's computers and that is true even in 4yr old laptops.

---

### Post by Onyros on 2008-12-21
I'm somewhat sad to say that I'm no longer on the Thinkpad exclusive club, I switched to a netbook and sold my Thinkpad X31.

It was still working perfectly, the battery still gave me 3 hours of usage, but I fell for a netbook. I got the Asus N10JC first, had a few HDD problems which led to a refund and then got a rebadged MSI Wind - an Advent 4211c - instead.

I will dearly miss my X31, but I actually sold it for more than my Advent cost me, and I'm pretty satisfied with the latter.

Still, I would recommend to anyone looking out for a netbook to try and grab a used Thinkpad X31. Forget the X40's, the HDD is 1.8" which is too expensive to upgrade whenever you need to, plus the X31 has a dedicated graphics chip... plus, the X40's are more expensive.

I'll still try getting a used Thinkpad T23 - mine collapsed of old age and a broken motherboard. I just love those things.

### Post by Miguel on 2008-12-22
> **emergant said:**
> Thanks I have a toshiba with a busted hard to find 4200 1.8 hd so I've learnt this lesson :)  Size wont be important for web browsing though :)

Oh, yes, it does ;). Since the platters are the same size for a given form factor, the bigger the disk capacity is, the higher the data density. This allows higher throughput. This would explain why my 2.5" 250 Gb 5400 rpm HD has almost double values in hdparm -tT than my older 2.5" 60 Gb 5400 rpm HD

### Post by r m h on 2008-12-23
maxed out T42p, replaced well over a year ago with a maxed out T61p - both run Linux full-time.

best loved feature of the T42p was easily swapped main hard drives, plus a second drive in the multibay slot for the VMs.  worst feature of the T42p, only 2GB RAM max.

best loved feature of the T61p is the combination of performance and long battery life, SATA HDDs and if needed, boots XP VMs in about 10-15 seconds and shuts them down even faster.  worst feature of the T61p, it came with Vista pre-installed and maxes out at 4GB of RAM.

### Post by archer6 on 2008-12-23
> **rainwalker said:**
> I'm actually planning on getting a ThinkPad when I graduate (I'm a junior right now), but I'm not sure what to aim for. Not only am I trying to future-proof it as much as possible, but I would like some power in it because I may go into 3D/CAD-related stuff. I'm so lost between the different choices, and I'm torn between a ThinkPad or a Studio/XPS (Dell). 
I've heard nothing but praise for ThinkPads, so can anyone offer their advice and/or help me in my decision? At the moment I'm just trying to figure out a general price I'm saving for.
Also, do ThinkPads play nice with Linux? They seem to have a lot of non-standard features, and while I'll most likely dual-boot, it would be nice to have it as compatible as possible

ThinkPads are well known to be very Linux friendly. I've loaded Ubuntu 8.04 on 8 now, for myself and friends and each and every one has performed very well once completed. No extra work, tweaking or anything was required. It's an amazing combination that I believe you would really like. 

Dell on the other hand while not bad, is no where close to what one can do with a ThinkPad. Not only that, every single area of a ThinkPad is of high quality and very well thought out. For example ThinkPads have stainless steel hinges that are very robust and have a lot of friction so as to hold the display firmly when open so it does not wiggle. The durability factor of a ThinkPad is simply amazing. Very strong, no flex cases are the norm. 

If one does a very close and accurate price comparison of comparably configured machines, often the ***[Lenovo ThinkPad]("http://shop.lenovo.com/SEUILibrary/controller/e/web/LenovoPortal/en_US/catalog.workflow:expandcategory?current-catalog-id=12F0696583E04D86B9B79B0FEC01C087&current-category-id=8FA114A7D9FF4F38AE8E19B36EC665A7")*** is actually less expensive than the Dell. I would suggest either the T-Series or the W-Series. Typically the displays and keyboards on ThinkPads are far superior to the Dell components. Light leakage is not an issue on Lenovo's displays which is not the case with Dell. There are lot of little considerations that go into a ThinkPad which is a reflection of their years of experience and sticking to the same basic style of case. They now include very complete roll cage structures inside so as to strengthen the laptop immensely. I highly endorse ThinkPads. As a computer scientist, 3D designer, I've used their work station class machine as main work computer for the last decade and could not be happier.

Namely the hotkeys don't work ( this can probably be configured with a bit of extra work ) except the brightness ones but they are inverted ( not a problem at all ), fingerprint reader doesn't work ( haven't tried to enable it that much since I don't actually use it for anything...i like to type in my passwords :) ) had some problems with the camera ( to be fair ubuntu installation is only two days old....didn't need the camera yet for anything that much so that i was forced to enable it...easycam recognizes it but as i said haven't played with the setup very much ) and the volume keys don't work. Here is my configuration 

Intel® Core 2 Duo Processor P7370 2.00GHz 3MBL2 1.066MHz

ThinkPad UltraNav / 56K V.92 designed modem

Windows XP Professional installed / Windows Vista Business license

Warranty 1 Year

it was the only one I could get where I live and no matter how hard i tried I couldn't get it without XP on it. so far it's amazing except those minor things i mentioned earlier but they pose no problem for me. haven't tried the HDMI but as far as i can tell people didn't have any problems with it. bluetooth and wifi work flawlessly, graphic drivers installed without a problem and the keyboard is awesome. some things will probably work better on 9.04 and probably already work better with 8.10 but I'll stick with my trusty 8.04 for now. still haven't tweaked it to maximize the battery life but nevertheless i get a 2.0 + hours of battery life. All in all it was a great buy, i have no regrets :)

p.s. debian works as equally awesome but requires little more effort :)

### Post by dkitty on 2009-01-21
I hope you don't mind a few notes. I like this thread and want to add a bit.

The Lenovo desktops used by my employer are okay. They are sturdy, solid, and the cases usually offer enough room for upgrades or repairs, but nothing to make me sing otherwise. Lenovo laptops are a different story.

I never really understood the draw to IBM stuff before this job. I used HP, Sony, and a few other brands. With the exception of a very happy Apple purchase, I usually bought whatever was cheap at the time. However, after supporting about 1k employees using Lenovo laptops, I have changed tunes.

My company does a fair bit of business with Zones in Seattle. They were nice enough to ship us the new T400 laptop and x200 tablet so we could create disk images for cloning. I played with both for about two weeks now (maybe a little less?).

I dig the widescreen on the x200. Getting the x200 to run Slackware was not fun. I had some success with a live CD of Puppy Linux. It had only a little audio glitch with Ubuntu. I eventually restored it from a backup with XP before building the clone image.

In my opinion, the x200 is SUCH an improvement over the x41, though I think I really prefer the x61. With the exception of the occasional video card glitch and a doctor slamming one of the tablets in frustration, the x62s have been bulletproof. The wireless hardware is spot on.

I fell in love with the T400, especially after pulling it apart. I drooled signifigantly until I saw the S10. If I had the $$ on-hand for the T400 when I bought the S10... nah, I still would have bought the S10. The decision would have been tough though.

### Post by Pawel Andrzej on 2009-02-19
I own a ThinkPad R40 (2897-B4U) and, like many here, love my machine. 

Here are some details from Lenovo website:
P M 1.4GHz, 256MB RAM, 40GB 4200rpm HDD, 15 XGA(1024x768) TFT LCD, 32MB ATI Radeon 7500, 24x10x24x/8x CD-RW/DVD, Intel 802.11b wireless(MPCI), Modem(CDC), 10/100 Ethernet(LOM), UltraNav, Secure Chip(TCPA), IEEE 1394, 8 cell Li-Ion battery, WinXP & Ubuntu 8.10 (Intrepid)

The Thinkpad was lent to me as part of an undergraduate fellowship deal, and became mine years later, once I completed the fellowship. At first, I thought it ugly and clunky. The screen lacked gloss, the red button in the middle of the keyboard seemed awkward, and the matte plastic casing made the computer look hopelessly dull. Still, I thought, it must have been better than nothing (back then, I could not afford to buy a laptop myself.)

As the years went by, however, I found beauty on the inside. The machine ran vector and graphic software without trouble, withstood serious falls without visible damage, made typing a pleasure, and received Linux with ease. I even came to appreciate the matte screen for its low glare, and the track-point for allowing to point with the hands still on the keyboard. I've had this ThinkPad since 2004, and it works just as well today as it did the day I received it, with the exception of the CD-RW/DVD drive, which died a couple of years ago, and which I never bothered to replace.

The R40 is a little on the heavy side, especially if one is to carry books with it, and when the time to upgrade comes, I will go with an X-series model; But it wouldn't even cross my mind to buy something other than a ThinkPad, so reliable and comfortable is the one I have.

### Post by Miguel on 2009-02-20
> **Dragonbite said:**
> I am using a T61 for work. Are you saying the non-T61 has a better screen?  That may have been a factor for my comments (waaaay above ;) ) regarding the screen brightness.

From what I've heard, screen brightness has more or less sucked in almost every thinkpad until the LED-backlit models. BUT what archer6 is saying is that IBM (and later lenovo) offered IPS screens up to the T60, with a PR name of FlexView. It's possible that 4:3 T61s also had the IPS screen option, but none of the current models offer an IPS screen.

99% of the laptop screens and a similar percentage of sub $300 desktop LCDs use a technology called TN (from Twisted Nematic). This technology is the cheapest way of creating an LCD, but only offers 18 bits of colour (at best) and has pretty poor viewing angles. IPS means In-plane switching, and is the technology used in **all** high-end monitors (i.e. Apple Cinema Displays, Dell 2408 FPW). This technology has much much better quality: less grain, at least 24 bit colour, no loss of image quality even at wild angles. Its two main disadvantages are price and being slightly slower than TN panels, so they aren't the best option for gaming.

More info in [wikipedia](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/TFT_LCD)

---

### Post by archer6 on 2009-02-20
> **Dragonbite said:**
> I am using a T61 for work. Are you saying the non-T61 has a better screen?  That may have been a factor for my comments (waaaay above ;) ) regarding the screen brightness.
First we should separate the topic of display brightness (level of back lighting) from the topic of the display technology itself. After all they are two completely different topics. 

1) Display technology is best explained in depth [**here**]("http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/TFT_LCD")

I will summarize it from a users standpoint. I have owned and used nearly every brand of laptop ever made. I find ThinkPads the very best. At the end of the day, the quality of the Non-IPS displays in ThinkPads has always been very good until approx 2007. With the intro of newer models by Lenovo some of the displays have been less than ideal. Prior to this two types of displays were offered. On the 15" T series in 2006 for example, one could order a T60 in a 15" size with the IPS technology (Flexview - model name) display. They were not available in the 14" size. Beginning with the T61 series IPS displays were no longer available, as the supplier discontinued them. Speaking from first hand use of IPS in my T43p, then T60p workstation class ThinkPads, these displays are simply stunning. They have a very wide viewing angle and the color reproduction is outstanding. The crisp hi-res images, fonts, lines etc all appear incredibly clear. Far and away the best display in my personal opinion. 

2) Back Lighting

IPS displays (and non IPS) were back lit with CCFL technology and were rated at 200 nits. This is more than bright enough, in fact I typically turn the illumination level of mine by about 20%. For many years engineers such as myself have used ThinkPads as the industry standard high end laptop, with great success. In more recent times the introduction of LED back lighting, along with glossy displays has given the typical laptop a different look. Many people are drawn to super bright, super glossy displays. As an owner of a brand new 300 nit MacBook Pro with glass display, (as my secondary machine) I find it very uncomfortable to work with on a daily basis. There is so much reflection and glare off the display that it's more like a mirror. And I'm not bashing the Mac, as I knew going in what I had selected and simply hoped that I could live with it, as it was the only choice, they do not offer a anti-glare matte display. At 300 nits, I must turn it down, near the 60% level as its' simply too bright. However it also must be noted that I have 20/20 vision. For those who have poor vision, I can see where the a very bright display may be useful.

In terms of regular TN displays from one brand to the next, IBM/Lenovo uses very high quality units, lit with high quality CCFL's and that makes quite a difference when one compares them to the competition. The even lighting from side to side, top to bottom, corner to corner is excellent. I have one of the new XPS Dell laptops and it's display is no match for any ThinkPad. Finally, my X301 ThinkPad is TN but back lit via LED rated at 300 nits. Again, just like my new Glass & Aluminum MacBook Pro, I must turn down the brightness on the X301 as it's simply too much. However it's very nice to work with as it has the typical ThinkPad anti-glare matte finish which eliminates any annoying glare or reflections.

---

### Post by fluxlizard on 2009-03-09
> Hello Fluxlizard, I have the same machien - or actually, my father had one lying around unused for who knows how many years...at least five. I figured I'd take it and turn it into a kids' computer for me, put in Edubuntu etc. probably. Just ordered a new cmos battery and some extra memory for it (it only came with 64 MB, I order a 256 Mb stick to go along it). Anyway: would you mind saying what you for network connection? Do you just use the wired lan or have you found a PCMCIA wlan card that works?

For wireless I've used a couple of different wireless cards. Both worked fine, but I broke the first one physically when mishandling. The card sticks out the side of the notebook, so removing when transporting is a good idea lol. 

The first might have been a linksys- can't remember for sure but I think so- few years back now. The one I have now is a Belkin wireless g card. Works great. 

I think there is a native driver that can be set up for it when pclinuxos gnome edition is run the first time, but I'm pretty sure I set it to run with ndiswrapper which was also very easy to set up- you just get the needed file off the install cd. ndiswrapper has the lights working properly on the card. The native linux driver works fine but no lights come on. Ndiswrapper is very easy to set up in the pclinuxos gnome edition control center. It sees the card automatically and it prompts you for the needed file and it's very easy to find on the cd that comes with the wireless card.

Both cards I chose just because they were cheap at the local walmart - I think the belkin was $35. Thanks to ndiswrapper I didn't have to go out of the way to select a card that would work. They work just fine and are fast, etc.

I can do pretty much anything on the old laptop that I can do on a modern computer, except 3d games (4mb ram video card doesn't cut it lol). Wesnoth, flobopuyo, etc, work great though, and for kids mame, dosbox, etc work great. So do linux edu-games like gcomprix. PCLinuxOS is about to come out with a new edition any month now and so the repos are frozen and a few months old at the moment, but I made my own packages from source and am still running the latest firefox, open office, gimp, etc and all works fine and comfortably fast.

### Post by majabl on 2009-03-16
> **hhhugues said:**
> just got an old t40 on ebay, cant wait to receive it! i want to try and run it on double boot xp / ubuntu. any issues likely to arise when doing that, i.e wireless not working or anything else, any easy ways around them ?

Watch out for the hidden partition that will have the data for you to format/reinstall XP on it, which might cause issues with your mbr!  I made that mistake with my T40, so ended up forking out £20 for the recovery CDs (which don't come with T40s, from what I can gather).

I don't remember *exactly* what the issue is, but if you search around a bit you ought to find what you need.  [http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=662620](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=662620) might be a good starting point for you, as it seems to be for the same issue I experienced.

Anyway, happy reading, and good luck!

### Post by archer6 on 2009-03-18
> **era86 said:**
> I'm curious as to why it is so cheap.  Possibly coming out with something new in the coming months.
The discount is to clear out their stock as they are no longer making models with the convention 4:3 display size. The new models are already out and being sold, known as the X200T with a widescreen. Despite all the ThinkPad users that prefer 4:3 format, the LCD manufacturers are only making widescreens so Lenovo had to follow that trend. 

Cheers...

### Post by archer6 on 2009-03-19
Actually I like both formats, as they each have their distinct advantages. While I greatly prefer the 4:3 format for work, its' mainly because I do a lot of 3D design, and other scientific work where its very advantageous to have as much vertical height from my display as possible. However when it comes to using one for my personal work I like the widescreen as I can put tool bars and panels on the sides due to the extra width of the widescreen format. So at the end of the day I find uses for both, and only wish that 4:3 was not being discontinued as I've used that on my workstation class ThinkPads for years. Once I no longer have that choice it will make my work harder. Such is the price one pays for "progress". 

Cheers...

---

### Post by ponchojuan on 2009-04-15
I have a Thinkpad 240.  A couple of mods made a huge difference.  I increased memory to 320MB, yes you can, there is a 256MB nine chip mem-config that works great and makes a big difference with Ubuntu.  I also replaced the Travelstar disk with a new 20GB Seagate,  What a difference; no noise and great throughput.
Installing without a bootable CD is a real pain in the ***.  I eventually got a network install to work from Unbootin, but it was painful.  The PCMCIA CDROM still does not work and no good answers here since the PCMCIA driver architecture is new.  My guess is it will eventually be solved.  The system works pretty well.

### Post by fsando on 2009-04-18
> **hhhugues said:**
> hi, totally different matter here. 
i'm running the ubuntu 9.04 beta but i'm completely new to linux. initially i was hoping to run it on double boot with windows xp, but after the installation going pear shaped and me not having the right original xp boot cd, i ended up reinstalling ubuntu from a completely outdated version of xp that doesnt even support wireless - meaning i cant go online with windows and therefore can't update it. 
anyway, i'm getting acquainted to ubuntu, which i like a lot, but theres things i don't know how to do yet. in particular, i'd like to reinstall the tpfan software i had on windows to regulate the fan speed as it can be noisy, but as much as it was straight forward for windows, i just don't understand how to do it for ubuntu. 
can anyone help ?
also, can i use windows to access the files i download and save with ubuntu on the computer? that could help putting my windows back into shape before reattempting a dual installation... sorry thats all over the place, but any help is more than welcome.

thanks
hugues

Hi hugues
I can't answer all your questions but I can tell you how to get your downloads from ubuntu into windows.

You have three options:
1)
You could simply use an external usb flashdrive. 

2)
From Ubuntu you can directly mount you windows partition and copy the files you want into windows. Look under "Places" in the menu for your windows partition. Just make sure you don't copy things into some of the system folders unless you know what you are doing.
If the option is not available you need to install ntfs-3g in Synaptic.

3)
You could make a new partition on your harddisk and make it readable from both OSs (make it's file system ntfs or fat32).

In Ubuntu start partition editor
System > Administration > Partition Editor
(if it's not there you need to install it in Synaptic)

In this program you can see your harddisk partitions. First you must resize one of your existing partitions to make room for the new one, then you can create a new partition in the empty space.
Make it readable from windows by making it fat32 or ntfs.
If the ntfs-option is not available you need to install ntfs-3g (and maybe ntfsprogs?) in Synaptic.

After that you can use that disk as a common ground for both OSs. That's how I have my machine. I have a common partition (ntfs) at about 15Gb with stuff I may need on both platforms.

Cheers

---

### Post by chili555 on 2009-04-20
My very first laptop was an ancient, yet rock-solid T23 bought on Ebay. I used it for years and decided to get my very first store-bought new computer, a T60. I had a new 100GB drive ready to go and ripped out the Windows drive immediately. I need my Windows drive a few times a year to figure my taxes and set up my Harmony universal remote. Ubuntu runs perfectly for everything I want to do. I have never had a need for and so never fine-tuned suspend/hibernate/cryo-sleep.

I sent my T23 to my sister who still uses it with PClinuxOS.

I saw a T40 on Ebay for super-cheap because it had no harddrive or power supply. From my T23 days, I happened to have just the parts I needed. I replaced the Aironet card with an Intel 2915ABG card. Ubuntu Jaunty works perfectly on the T40 as well.

A few days ago, I was researching T43's on Ebay and they're getting pretty cheap these days for a pretty capable machine if you want internet, email, instant messaging, document creation, photo editing and the simple stuff.

### Post by ohbuntu on 2009-04-20
I am a relative newbie to the world of Thinkpads and bought my first back during graduate school. It was a T40 but since then, I've used about 5 Thinkpads (T42, T43, T60, Z61, T400 and an X200 that I purchased recently). The T42's USB ports don't work, no thanks to the well-known ESD problem, yet runs 24*7 at home for downloading movies and other stuff while the X200 and T400 are my trusty sidekicks depending on whether I need to catch an airplane or go to the office.

TBH, I think the T4x era was the greatest of all. All the newer Thinkpads, right from the T60 to the current T400 seem to be missing some elements of that Thinkpad magic.

I really hope Lenovo get the more powerful P9xxx and T9550 on the options sheet very soon. Either that, or some low powered quad cores. :D

### Post by archer6 on 2009-04-22
> **CharmyBee said:**
> does 760XL count as X?
No, the 760 is not an X model. All X models have an X prior to the model number. Such as X30, X40, X200 etc. 

To the best of my knowledge there is no such model as a 760XL. However there are several 760 models. Here is a link for you to look them up. 

[http://www.thinkwiki.org/wiki/ThinkPad_History](http://www.thinkwiki.org/wiki/ThinkPad_History)

Cheers...

### Post by system11 on 2009-05-19
Just a note for anyone considering a W700 - it's far from smooth sailing in Linux.

So far: 

Ubuntu 9.04 - need to use the alternate install disk if you're using the Intel RAID setup.  Screen brightness adjustment doesn't work!  Get lots of phantom freezing during boot and operation, which is cleared by ... pressing a key (or similar).  That last part appears do be down to interrupts, setting 'noapic' appears (so far) to fix it.

Fedora 11 (preview) - spots the disks straight from boot, and the screen brightness works (maybe an Ubuntu maintainer could try to find out why Fedora's works).  Again, suffers from random interrupt related freezing which 'noapic' on boot fixes.

I'm trying to find a BIOS way of fixing the interrupt issue, I notice an awful lot of things are set to 11 in the settings, but so far only noapic has made the machine run correctly.

---

Get lots of phantom freezing during boot and operation, which is cleared by ... pressing a key (or similar).  That last part appears do be down to interrupts, setting 'noapic' appears (so far) to fix it.

Crap...I'm going to get a W700 this summer. That's not encouraging at all :(

### Post by system11 on 2009-05-19
> **rainwalker said:**
> Crap...I'm going to get a W700 this summer. That's not encouraging at all :(

I'm guessing the pausing is caused by this open kernel bug:

[http://bugzilla.kernel.org/show_bug.cgi?id=11727](http://bugzilla.kernel.org/show_bug.cgi?id=11727)

I have since found that "clocksource=jiffies" seems to make it work too, although I haven't done much testing past that.  The screen brightness I can't get to work at all in Ubuntu, no idea where to start really, I'll probably end up using Fedora 11 when it comes out because at defaults the screen is way too bright.

### Post by archer6 on 2009-05-19
I installed Ubuntu 9.04 on my W700, and everything worked perfectly without any tweaks required. 

I am so impressed at how well 9.04 works on my ThinkPads, as this is the third one I've done. I have it on my T60, my X301, and now this W700. Previously I had 8.04 on the T60 and X301, and while both of those also went well, I'd read about wireless problems with 9.04 and yet I had no problem with mine. All three machines are very stable, fast and fun. 

It took less than an hour to install 9.04 on the W700 and I could not be happier. 

Here is the configuration for my W700. I have Ubuntu 9.04 on the first hard drive, and Windows 7 on the second hard drive. 

	ThinkPad W700 
              2757-CTO
              Processor: Intel® Core&#8482; 2 Duo processor T9600 (2.8GHz 1066MHz 6MBL2) 		
	Operating system: Genuine Windows Vista Business 				
	Operating system language: Genuine Windows Vista Business US English 			
	Display type: 17" WXGA+ LCD 200NIT 				
	System Graphics: NVIDIA Quadro FX 2700M 48-core CUDA parallel computing processor 512MB (dedicated) 		
	Total memory: 8 GB PC3-8500 DDR3 SDRAM 1067MHz SODIMM Memory (2 DIMM) 		
	Keyboard: Keyboard US English  							
	Pointing Device: Ultranav + Fingerprint Reader 						
	Primary Hard Drive: Non-RAID HDD, 250 GB Hard Disk Drive, 7200rpm with Disk Encryption 			
	Second Hard Drive: Non-RAID HDD, 250 GB Hard Disk Drive, 7200rpm with Disk Encryption

### Post by Miguel on 2009-06-20
> **Gucko said:**
> 
I heard many people say that, why?? What's wrong with the new ones?
I want to get one, but I'm not sure after I heard this.

Some people said bad reviews about T400/500's keyboard. 

I don't know what's wrong with the new ones. Should I consider Dell instead of ThinkPad now?

What I like most about ThinkPads are the Matte screens. I hate glossy screens, they hurt my eyes.

I suppose the main issue regarding the quality of the new ThinkPads is akin to the placebo effect. Switch from IBM to Lenovo may have put some people ultradefensive regarding the quality of their laptops. Furthermore, average build quality of laptops has increased considerably, thus making the flaws of thinkpads more obvious.

It's true that quite a few people complained about the **original** T400 keyboard, albeit I am extremely satisfied with mine (first thinkpad, though). In order to save weight, it seems that Lenovo drilled the metallic base of the keyboard. However, current production T400 have reverted to the T61 keyboard, if that makes you happier.

When I bought my T400 back in November 2008, I was in doubt between a Dell E6400 and a T400. I finally went to the thinkpad because the pictures of some reviews of the Dell showed some build flaws, and the thinkpad I got (not the perfect one [1]) cost me 1250€ (16% tax already included). Had I gone for the Dell, I wouldn't have picked without all I wanted, and that would have cost me at least 300€ more[2].

Depending on what country you are, not getting the "default" model is extremely expensive. We don't have CTO models here in Spain, and have to swim in a sea of preconfigured models in order to get the one you want. These are all premium-priced. Getting the 99% Thinkpad I wanted, instead of the 95% one I finally got would have cost me nearly 700€. It's silly.

[1] I wanted a P9500 processor (got the 8600 instead), didn't care about hybrid graphics (which I got) and because the windows tax was unavoidable I wanted 64bit Vista (I got 32 bit Vista downgraded to XP, where hybrid graphics doesn't work). I was unable to get the LED screen WXGA+ screen, and had to conform with a CCFL WXGA+ screen. I don't know the current state of affairs now, but 10 months ago it was almost impossible to get a LED backlit screen here in Spain.

[2] The Dell wouldn't have DDR3 memory, and I was positive I didn't want the nVidia GPU, whose real cost due to Dell's curious upgrade policy only costed 50€ from the configuration I desired. It would have used the LED screen, though.

Hi
I've owned a Dell xps1330, two different asus and a fujitsu-siemens, and now a T500 for a about 8 months and I have pretty close contact with a macbook pro. In my opinion Lenovo beats them all. They all have some area where they are pretty good and areas where they fall through. Lenovo seems to be equal or a tad better in all areas and it has no real bad side to it. It feels like Lenovo is really build to last and to be functional in a no-nonsense way I haven't experienced before.

Actually it reminds me of when I worked as a sound engineer and worked with professional sound equipment. The difference between Lenovo and the other laptops reminds of the difference between professional and consumer equipment. In consumer equipment you have a lot apparent functionality designed to lure consumers and it is designed to fail just after the warranty expires. In professional equipment all functionality has a purpose and you can't afford that it fails.

The only possible downside is that (in the eyes of some) it is not exactly beautiful.

### Post by majabl on 2009-06-21
> **Miguel said:**
> However, current production T400 have reverted to the T61 keyboard, if that makes you happier.

I've got a T40 laptop for myself and a T61 laptop for work.  The T61's keyboard is pretty good, but it's noticeably less good than the one on the T40.  If I could have the T40's keyboard on the T61, I'd be a happier typist!

---

### Post by ohbuntu on 2009-06-21
> **majabl said:**
> I've got a T40 laptop for myself and a T61 laptop for work.  The T61's keyboard is pretty good, but it's noticeably less good than the one on the T40.  If I could have the T40's keyboard on the T61, I'd be a happier typist!

Well, that depends on what keyboard you have on your T40. Thinkpads have three different types of keyboards, viz., Alps, Chicony and NMB. Based on my experience of using all three, NMB's are usually the best, followed by Alps and Chicony (YMMV). You can usually identify the type of keyboard on your laptop by looking up the keyboard FRU for your system on the IBM parts lookup web site and then matching that with the keyboard KB.

It's possible that you have the NMB on your T40 and a clickety Chicony on the T61. If the T61 is still under warranty, try and order a replacement NMB keyboard.

---

### Post by ohbuntu on 2009-06-22

Sure, here you go:
[http://www-307.ibm.com/pc/support/site.wss/document.do?lndocid=LOOK-WARNTY](http://www-307.ibm.com/pc/support/site.wss/document.do?lndocid=LOOK-WARNTY)

You'll need to input the machine type and serial number that are printed on the bottom panel of your Thinkpad. Click on "Continue" and then click on "View parts lookup results for this system" on the ensuing page.

The keyboard conversation has gotten out of hand, as the early T400's did have keyboards that operated just fine, and were stiffer than most laptops, but less stiff than the prior T series. So Lenovo took care of that. They did _not_ go to a T61 keyboard as some people as guessing. 

I've had both the first production T400 and the newer T400 with the improved keyboard mounting, and the new ones are every bit as good as my older ThinkPads. 

Overall the Lenovo ThinkPads are actually better than the IBM models I have since they have rollcages now, and a host of other improvements. The reason people say the IBM ThinkPads are better is because those are the people that cannot accept change and improvements without being critical. 

I've used a ThinkPad everyday, for the last decade for my work and upgrade to the latest model at every cycle. I've had excellent service from ThinkPads the entire time.

### Post by archer6 on 2009-06-22
> **ohbuntu said:**
>  If the T61 is still under warranty, try and order a replacement NMB keyboard.
For clarification, the ThinkPad warranty is the same as any other product warranty. The purpose of the warranty is to fix or replace a faulty part, or problem that you are having with your computer. Since Lenovo (like other manufacturers) uses a few different vendors for parts like keyboards, you are not given a choice of what brand. If yours is defective then they will replace it, but if yours is not defective, but you simply want one made by a different company you must pay for it. The keyboard you send in will be inspected and if it's not defective you will be charged for the new keyboard. There is no cheating. Lenovo's too smart to allow that. 

I've had all three manufacturers keyboards over the years and find them all very nice. They are simply different. The myth that NMB is the best, is just that... a myth. Some people like keyboards that are softer and totally silent. Others prefer keyboards with audible & tactile feedback. The early Chicony keyboards (circa 1999) had a noisy mechanism. The newer ones (circa 2002 & newer) while still audible are very good. All three brands are still far better than any other notebook maker uses. 

Cheers...

---

### Post by ohbuntu on 2009-06-23
> **majabl said:**
> Thanks!

The T40 has an 08K5046, apparently, and the T61's is a 42T3233.  What these digits translate to I can't find!
Aha! I was right in my assumption at the start!

42T3233 = U.K. English Alps keyboard as specified in the keyboard KB out [HERE]("http://www-307.ibm.com/pc/support/site.wss/MIGR-68225.html").

08K5046 = U.K. English NMB keyboard as seen out [HERE]("http://www-307.ibm.com/pc/support/site.wss/document.do?lndocid=MIGR-46503").

[quote=archer6]
For clarification, the ThinkPad warranty is the same as any other product warranty. The purpose of the warranty is to fix or replace a faulty part, or problem that you are having with your computer. Since Lenovo (like other manufacturers) uses a few different vendors for parts like keyboards, you are not given a choice of what brand. If yours is defective then they will replace it, but if yours is not defective, but you simply want one made by a different company you must pay for it. The keyboard you send in will be inspected and if it's not defective you will be charged for the new keyboard. There is no cheating. Lenovo's too smart to allow that.[/quote]
Agreed but it also depends on your persuasion skills. I've had two broken keyboards in the past and have always called the customer support folks based in Atlanta. Those guys really know how to please a customer and generally accede to my request. Unfortunately, the ginormous scale of logistics at Lenovo means that ordering a specific part # is not always possible, so if you were to receive a different keyboard, you can simply return it and ask for another one, based on some simple reasoning and thought. Of course, I don't go about ordering keyboards and other parts every month and certainly do not recommend that anyone do so.

[quote=archer6]
I've had all three manufacturers keyboards over the years and find them all very nice. They are simply different. The myth that NMB is the best, is just that... a myth. Some people like keyboards that are softer and totally silent. Others prefer keyboards with audible & tactile feedback. The early Chicony keyboards (circa 1999) had a noisy mechanism. The newer ones (circa 2002 & newer) while still audible are very good. All three brands are still far better than any other notebook maker uses.[/quote]
That's precisely why I wrote "YMMV" in my earlier post. You might find solace with either of the three keyboards but I just can't. If you aren't a heavy typist who bangs out documents and e-mails all day, there's a good chance you won't really find fault with either keyboard model. To each his own, I guess.
